<p>The <b>long-eared owl</b> (<i>Asio otus</i>), also known as the <b>northern long-eared owl</b><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> or, more informally, as the <b>lesser horned owl</b> or <b>cat owl</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> is a medium-sized species of <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> with an extensive breeding range. The scientific name is from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>. The genus name <i>Asio</i> is a type of eared owl, and <i>otus</i> also refers to a small, eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-job_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-job-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The species breeds in many areas through <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. This species is a part of the larger grouping of owls known as <a href="/wiki/Typical_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Typical owl">typical owls</a>, of the family <a href="/wiki/Strigidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Strigidae">Strigidae</a>, which contains most extant species of owl (while the other taxonomic family of owls are the <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Tytonidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Tytonidae">Tytonidae</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This owl shows a partiality for semi-open habitats, particularly <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">woodland edge</a>, as they prefer to roost and nest within <a href="/wiki/Grove_(nature)" title="Grove (nature)">dense stands of wood</a> but prefer to hunt over <a href="/wiki/Open_terrain" title="Open terrain">open ground</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The long-eared owl is a somewhat specialized predator, focusing its diet almost entirely on small <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, which quite often compose most of their diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Under some circumstances, such as <a href="/wiki/Population_cycle" title="Population cycle">population cycles</a> of their regular prey, <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Insular_biogeography" title="Insular biogeography">insular</a> regional habitats or <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a>, this species can adapt fairly well to a diversity of prey, including <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kiat_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiat-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Trujillo_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> All owls do not build their own nests. In the case of the long-eared owl, the owls generally utilizes nests that are built by other animals, with a partiality in many regions for those built by <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success in this species is largely correlated with prey populations and predation risks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Unlike many owls, long-eared owls are not strongly territorial nor sedentary. They are partially <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a> and, although owls appear to generally use the same migratory routes and wintering sites annually, can tend to appear so erratically that they are sometimes characterized as “nomadic”.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston2-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Another fairly unique characteristic of this species is its partially for regular roosts that are often shared by a number of long-eared owls at once.<sup id="cite_ref-Pirovano_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pirovano-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The long-eared owl is one of the most widely distributed and most numerous owl species in the world, and due to its very broad range and numbers it is considered a <a href="/wiki/Least_concern_species" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern species">least concern species</a> by the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>. Nonetheless, strong declines have been detected for this owl in several parts of its range.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_(24684373661).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg/220px-Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg/330px-Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg/440px-Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_%2824684373661%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="2228" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-Eared_Owl_on_Seedskadee_NWR_(24684373661).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-eared owl at Seedskadee National Wildlife Refuge (Wyoming)</div></div></div>
<p>This species is a rather slim and long winged owl with usually prominent erectile ear tufts, which are positioned closer to the center of the head than in many other types of owl. The purposes of ear tufts are not definitively known and are present in about half of living owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Arguably the most popular theory amongst <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ornithologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornithologist">ornithologists</a> is that ear tufts could be a means of <a href="/wiki/Intra-species_recognition" title="Intra-species recognition">intraspecies communication</a> of intent and mood.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinbach_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steinbach-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In general coloration, the long-eared owl is often considered a hue of <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochraceous</a>-<a href="/wiki/Tawny_(color)" title="Tawny (color)">tawny</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Grey" title="Grey">grayish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brown" title="Brown">brownish</a> wash variably manifesting. The base color is commonly overlaid with variable <a href="/wiki/Black" title="Black">blackish</a> vertical streaks (and occasionally spots), which are usually more apparent about the wings and back. The <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapulars</a> are usually marked <a href="/wiki/White" title="White">whitish</a>, which provide further contrast when seen against the base color and blackish markings. The wing's dark carpal patches can also display broad panels of <a href="/wiki/Buff_(colour)" title="Buff (colour)">buff</a> or almost <a href="/wiki/Orange_(colour)" title="Orange (colour)">orange</a> on the wings across the base of primaries, which represent a more richly emphasized version of a pattern shared with other owls that tend to be <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">vole</a>-hunting specialists, like <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Great_gray_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Great gray owl">great gray owl</a> (<i>Strix nebulosa</i>). On the underside, the body tends to be a somewhat paler ochraceous-tawny compared to the upperside. Long-eared owls tend to have <a href="/wiki/Dusk" title="Dusk">dusky</a> streaks on the upper breast, below which they may be heavily marked with herring bone pattern (which is created by dusky shaft-streaks and crossbars on these feathers).<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> There is much individual and regional variation in markings with owls dwelling in more extensively forested regions tending to be of a darker hue, often so densely washed above as to appear largely dusky brown on the back and the underside largely overlaid with bolder dusky-blackish marks. Meanwhile, in some <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>-like regions, the plumage may tend towards a somewhat more washed out look, at times appearing fairly <a href="/wiki/Cream_(colour)" title="Cream (colour)">cream</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yellow" title="Yellow">yellowish</a>, with sparser and lighter dusky markings overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The facial disc is visibly well developed and variably colored (see subspecies) in the species, rimmed dusky often with white running down along the center through the bill, while at times the white lines form a “moustache” and/or extending to the inside of the facial disc rim. The ear tufts are usually dusky in front and paler tawny on the back.  Long-eared owl possess a blackish bill color while its eyes may vary from yellowish-orange to orange-red, tarsi and toes feathered.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The long-eared owl is a medium-sized owl, which measures between 31 and 40&#160;cm (12 and 16&#160;in) in total length.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume2_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume2-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Their wingspan is relatively large for their size, measuring 86 to 102&#160;cm (2&#160;ft 10&#160;in to 3&#160;ft 4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-SnakeRiver_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SnakeRiver-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sprunt_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sprunt-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> However, compared to other widespread owls considered of medium size and to which they can appear broadly similar in size, such as <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> (<i>Strix aluco</i>), the long-eared owl is quite a bit lighter and slenderer bodied, with mature weights around half of those of tawny owls not being uncommon.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Earhart_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earhart-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> As expected in owls and <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> in general, long-eared owls display <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">reverse sexual dimorphism</a> in which females are usually slightly larger than males (to the contrary of most non-raptorial birds). Males furthermore may tend to be somewhat paler in plumage than females.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Earhart_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earhart-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, one survey of the body mass of mature birds found that 22 males averaged 288&#160;g (10.2&#160;oz) while 20 females averaged 327&#160;g (11.5&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In body mass, European long-eared owls per a study were shown to run contrary to <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%27s_rule" title="Bergmann&#39;s rule">Bergmann's rule</a> (that widespread animals should be smaller closer to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>) as body mass seemed to increase further south, being lightest in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, where 37 males averaged 197&#160;g (6.9&#160;oz) and 24 females averaged 225&#160;g (7.9&#160;oz), intermediate in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and heaviest in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, where 21 males averaged 256&#160;g (9.0&#160;oz) and 24 females averaged 308&#160;g (10.9&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Erritzoe_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erritzoe-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In migratory owls largely from <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> summer grounds in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, the average weight of 8 males was 263.6&#160;g (9.30&#160;oz) and the average of 28 females was 294.7&#160;g (10.40&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Harvey_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvey-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, in various studies in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, 38 males (in <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>) were found to average 245.3&#160;g (8.65&#160;oz), 55 males (also Montana in a different study) to average 261&#160;g (9.2&#160;oz) and 15 males (from <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>) 232&#160;g (8.2&#160;oz). From the same studies, respectively, 28 females averaged 279.4&#160;g (9.86&#160;oz), 49 females averaged 337&#160;g (11.9&#160;oz) and 19 females averaged 288&#160;g (10.2&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Earhart_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earhart-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ulmschneider_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulmschneider-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Museum specimens in North America were found to average 245&#160;g (8.6&#160;oz) in 38 males and 279&#160;g (9.8&#160;oz) in 28 females, while 520 migrating adults in <a href="/wiki/Duluth" class="mw-redirect" title="Duluth">Duluth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> averaged 281.1&#160;g (9.92&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snyder_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In all, long-eared owl males may vary in weight from 160 to 330&#160;g (5.6 to 11.6&#160;oz) while females may vary from 180 to 435&#160;g (6.3 to 15.3&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Erritzoe_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erritzoe-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:B%C3%BAho_chico_(Asio_otus),_Arcos_de_la_Frontera,_C%C3%A1diz,_Espa%C3%B1a,_2015-12-08,_DD_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg/220px-B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg/330px-B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg/440px-B%C3%BAho_chico_%28Asio_otus%29%2C_Arcos_de_la_Frontera%2C_C%C3%A1diz%2C_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_2015-12-08%2C_DD_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4287" data-file-height="5288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:B%C3%BAho_chico_(Asio_otus),_Arcos_de_la_Frontera,_C%C3%A1diz,_Espa%C3%B1a,_2015-12-08,_DD_04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close-up of the head.</div></div></div>
<p>In standard measurements, long-eared owls vary in <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> length from 262 to 315&#160;mm (10.3 to 12.4&#160;in) in males, with 883 in North America averaging 285.1&#160;mm (11.22&#160;in), and 255 to 332&#160;mm (10.0 to 13.1&#160;in) in females, with 520 American ones averaging 286.2&#160;mm (11.27&#160;in). The <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> may measure 121.5 to 161&#160;mm (4.78 to 6.34&#160;in) in males and 143.5 to 165&#160;mm (5.65 to 6.50&#160;in) in females, with 1408 owls averaging 146.3&#160;mm (5.76&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ridgway_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ridgway-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Less widely measured are <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bill</a> length, which averaged 15.7&#160;mm (0.62&#160;in) in males and 16.2&#160;mm (0.64&#160;in) in females from North America, and <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> length, which averaged 38.2&#160;mm (1.50&#160;in) in 20 males and 39.9&#160;mm (1.57&#160;in) for 16 females from Europe, with a range in both sexes of 36.9 to 42.3&#160;mm (1.45 to 1.67&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Snyder_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AllAboutBirds_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AllAboutBirds-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In terms of their skeletal structure, it bears a relatively wide skull but relatively small eyes and orbits, while the <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> is relatively elongated but weak.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ridgway_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ridgway-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The legs are relatively long and thin and, while needle-sharp, the talons are relatively fine and the feet relatively weak for an owl. However, the talons are still highly proficient at drawing blood if contact is made with human skin.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ridgway_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ridgway-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Niedl_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Niedl-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Csermely_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Csermely-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identification">Identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/220px-Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/330px-Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg/440px-Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2550" data-file-height="3300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-eared_Owl_from_the_Crossley_ID_Guide_Britain_and_Ireland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A composite of images of long-eared owl for identification from the Crossley ID Guide Britain and Ireland.</div></div></div>
<p>If seen well, an experienced observer is usually able to distinguish a long-eared owl by combination of its field marks, size and coloration. However, some potential owl species may be confused for them. <a href="/wiki/Stygian_owl" title="Stygian owl">Stygian owls</a> (<i>Asio stygius</i>) (which barely overlap perhaps in northern Mexico) is larger with partially bare toes and generally darker with <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">inky</a> and more boldly patterned plumage, with often nearly the enter facial mask appearing off-black.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Howell_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Howell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">Tawny owls</a>, which co-exist with long-eared owls in Eurasia, are unlikely to be mistaken given that they usually appear considerably rounder and bulkier overall (and are indeed slightly larger and much heavier), further possessing a much broader, more rounded head. The tawny species has no ear tufts, eyes of blackish-brown colour and relatively shorter wings. In flight, tawny owls show well-fingered primaries (with five apparent emarginations) unlike the squared off wings of the long-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) is far larger and more massive than a long-eared owl with visibly more overdeveloped and  powerful-looking feet and talons and a huge squared-off-looking head with the ear tufts set nearer to the edge. The eagle-owl is often more heavily patterned on the crown and back with heavy blackish marking but has a less strongly demarked (and shallower) facial disc when compared to the long-eared.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of the wintering range, other <a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">eagle-owls</a> may scarcely abut the wide range of long-eared owls in Eurasia (and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">Northwestern Africa</a>) but are usually distinguishable (similarly as in the Eurasian species) by size differences, plumage characteristics and, occasionally, habitat preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Asian <a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">fish owls</a>, which are essentially a subset of eagle-owls, are generally also much larger than long-eared owls with tousled looking ear tufts, have less variable coloring and often have feathering over only part of their tarsi.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In North America, <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owls</a> (<i>Bubo virginianus</i>), yet another type of eagle-owl in all but name, have a squarish head and more widely separated ear tufts. Like other <i>Bubo</i> species, great horned owls are also perceptibly larger and more massively built than any long-eared owl (despite being smaller than the Eurasian eagle-owl). Great horned owls also have typically heavily barred, rather than streaked, underparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl">Scops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Screech_owl" title="Screech owl">screech owls</a> are much smaller than long-eared owls, as well as differently marked (often with more individual varying overall coloring, i.e. from gray to brown to <a href="/wiki/Rufous" title="Rufous">rufous</a>) and usually have rather short ear tufts.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marsh_owl" title="Marsh owl">Marsh owl</a> (<i>Asio capensis</i>) (rare overlap perhaps in northern <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>) is generally brown with quite different looking fine mottling or barring below and has brown eyes and tiny ear tufts.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Over much of its range, long-eared owls occur with the related <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a>, the latter species averaging slightly larger. Much has been written about distinguishing them in the field, though, if seen well, the resemblance between the species is not particularly strong. Furthermore, the two species differ in habitat preferences, with the short-eared owl often favoring (where available) any kind of entirely treeless, open habitats (including many <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> and Arctic <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairie</a> and extensive <a href="/wiki/Meadow" title="Meadow">meadows</a>), often avoiding the edge habitats favored by the long-eared.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> However, in dim light, at a distance or in flight, confusion is certainly possible.<sup id="cite_ref-Davis_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In both long-eared and short-eared owls, the flight style when seen has a distinctive, erratic and <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyant</a> flapping quality that many <a href="/wiki/Birdwatcher" class="mw-redirect" title="Birdwatcher">birdwatchers</a> considered reminiscent of a <a href="/wiki/Moth" title="Moth">moth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> At rest, the ear-tufts of the long-eared owl serve to easily distinguish the two (although long-eared owls can sometimes hold their ear-tufts lax). The iris-colour differs: yellow in short-eared, and often orange in long-eared. Furthermore, the black surrounding the eyes is vertical and slight on the long-eared, and horizontal and far more conspicuous on the short-eared. Overall, the short-eared owl tends to be a paler, sandier-looking bird than the long-eared, lacking the darker and more extensive markings of the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  There are a number of other ways in which the two species differ which are best seen when they are flying. Short-eared owls often have a broad white band along the rear edge of the wing, which is not shown by long-eared owls. On the upperwing, the short-eared owl's primary-patches are usually paler and more obvious. The band on the upper side of the short-eared owl's tail are usually bolder than those of the long-eared. The short-eared's innermost secondaries are often dark-marked, contrasting with the rest of the underwing. The long-eared owl has streaking throughout its underparts whereas on the short-eared the streaking ends at the breast. The dark markings on the underside of the tips of the longest primaries are bolder on short-eared owls. The upperparts of short-eared owls are coarsely blotched, whereas on the long-eared they are more finely marked. The short-eared owl also differs structurally from the long-eared, having longer, slimmer wings. The shorter and broader wings in combination with a squarer tail in long-eared owls produce proportions more reminiscent of a <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">buzzard</a> than the short-eared.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis_37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robertson_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harris_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>  When studied by their <a href="/wiki/Osteology" title="Osteology">osteological features</a>, however, the long-eared and short-eared owls are difficult to distinguish.<sup id="cite_ref-Emslie_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emslie-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations_and_ear_morphology">Vocalizations and ear morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Vocalizations and ear morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg/220px-Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg/330px-Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg/440px-Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ear_of_Long-eared_Owl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Close up of the long-eared owls relatively large ears.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="60.36015" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Asio_otus_-_Long-eared_Owl_XC108293.mp3" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/39/Asio_otus_-_Long-eared_Owl_XC108293.mp3/Asio_otus_-_Long-eared_Owl_XC108293.mp3.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="108224" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Asio_otus_-_Long-eared_Owl_XC108293.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="Original MP3 file (320 kbps)" data-shorttitle="MP3 source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="320000" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_-_Long-eared_Owl_XC108293.mp3" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various vocalizations and auditory displays of long-eared owls.</div></div></div>
<p>The long-eared owl has relatively large <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">ear slits</a> placed <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetrically</a> on the sides of its head, as in a majority of owls, with the left ear higher and right lower in order to allow them to absorb sound both from above and below.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The ear slit very nearly occupy the full height of the skull, being are about 38&#160;mm (1.5&#160;in), long and covered in movable skin flaps.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Collett_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collett-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schwartzkopff_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartzkopff-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The right ear is about 13% larger based on freshly dead owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Norberg_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schwartzkopff2_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartzkopff2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Due to its ear structure, the hearing of a long-eared owl is around ten times better at hearing high and medium <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitches</a> than humans.<sup id="cite_ref-vanDijk_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanDijk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">Barn owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owl</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>) have (via <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>) roughly similar ear structures, with the relative size of the ear structure and facial disc in owls generally indicative of the level of importance of acute hearing to their life history. Owls with relatively smaller ear slits and shallower or vestigial facial discs tend to skew towards more <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a> or partially <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal</a> behaviors, whereas owls such as long-eared owls are more or less entirely nocturnal. It is well known that a majority of owls can hunt in darkness due to their extraordinary hearing, which allows them to pinpoint locations of prey, but they can also utilize their hearing to track intraspecific calls and activities and avoid predation risks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schwartzkopff2_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartzkopff2-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vanDijk_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanDijk-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The vocalizations of this species are highly variable. Amongst owls of all ages, long-eared owls in <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> were recorded to have made 23 different vocalizations. <a href="/wiki/Karel_Voous" title="Karel Voous">Karel Voous</a> considered them to likely be the most diverse vocalists of all owl species in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_hemisphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern hemisphere">Northern hemisphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The song of the male long-eared owl is a deep <i>whoop</i>, which is repeated at intervals of several seconds. It starts with some hoots at slightly lower pitch before reaching full <a href="/wiki/Loudness" title="Loudness">volume</a> and quality. On calm nights, this song may carry over up to 1 to 2&#160;km (0.62 to 1.24&#160;mi) away (at least to human auditory perception). The song of the male is around 400 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, some observers have considered the male's song as <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogous</a> to the deep cooing of <a href="/wiki/Band-tailed_pigeon" title="Band-tailed pigeon">band-tailed pigeons</a> (<i>Patagioenas fasciata</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Females gives a weaker, less clear and much higher pitched song with a nasal quality. In nearly all owl species, the females, despite being typically the larger sex, have smaller <a href="/wiki/Syrinx_(bird_anatomy)" title="Syrinx (bird anatomy)">syrinx</a> than the males and so tend to have less powerful voices.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schwartzkopff_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartzkopff-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The call of the female is at times compared to a weak <a href="/wiki/Tin_whistle" title="Tin whistle">tin whistle</a> and is only audible at close range, being about 4-5 <a href="/wiki/Halftone" title="Halftone">halftones</a> higher than the song of the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Females usually call only in duet with male during courtship, but also when the nest is selected and around the beginning of incubation (probably in conjunction with food-begging). In fact, close study has revealed that female calling may occur as frequently as every 2–8 seconds at times of night between nest selection and egg laying.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Collett_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collett-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Both sexes utter a <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cat</a>-like, somewhat hoarse <i>jaiow</i> notes or high <i>yip-yip</i> notes, the latter reminiscent of a call made by <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>. When disturbed near the nest holding young, both parents may utter a series of tinny tones, <i>watt-watt-watt-watt</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> During the period of courtship the male flies around and flaps its wing around, producing a clapping sound. During the display flight, the male may make as many as 20 claps.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> As with many owls, all ages may produce hissing sounds and bill snapping when they feel threatened, especially in the context of nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Fledging young call all with high-pitched, drawn-out notes, variously transcribed as <i>feek</i>, <i>peeyee</i> and <i>pzeei</i>, and are often likened to the noise of a <a href="/wiki/Gate" title="Gate">gate</a> swinging on a <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rusty</a> <a href="/wiki/Hinge" title="Hinge">hinge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The long-eared owl is a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i>, which are sometimes commonly referred to as eared owls. Despite the extensive modern distribution of the species, only seven modern species are thought to exist. Four species are found both in <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, including the long-eared owl and short-eared owl in both.<sup id="cite_ref-ioc_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Despite similarities and being considered as in the same genus, it was found in a study utilizing <a href="/wiki/Electrophoresis" title="Electrophoresis">electrophoresis</a> that the genetic distance between long-eared owls and <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> was unusually large for species within the same genera.<sup id="cite_ref-Randi_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randi-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Notwithstanding <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil records</a> of <i>Asio</i> species showing the species presence during prehistory in locations like <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> (<i>Asio brevipes</i>) and <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> (<i>Asio priscus</i>), the exact origins of the long-eared owl are unknown nor their area of evolutionary origin and are unlikely to ever be known.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ford_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> At least three modern species represent related derivations, possibly with long-eared owls as the <a href="/wiki/Paraspecies" title="Paraspecies">paraspecies</a> or with the long-eared as part of a <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> that potentially bear a <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randi_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randi-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In all three cases, the related owls are obviously more <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> in distribution and adapted to more humid conditions, with darker plumage and are larger bodied with apparently stronger feet and more overdeveloped talons, possibly exploiting a more or less unoccupied <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> against competing owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> One of these three is the <a href="/wiki/Stygian_owl" title="Stygian owl">Stygian owl</a>, which is the darkest derivation of all and readily known to be distinct for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The other two slightly larger, tropical species possibly housed in a <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> with the long-eared owls were at one time considered to be part of the long-eared owls species. One of these is the <a href="/wiki/Madagascan_owl" title="Madagascan owl">Madagascan owl</a> (<i>Asio madagascariensis</i>), obviously <a href="/wiki/Endemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic">endemic</a> to the island of its name, while the other is the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_owl" title="Abyssinian owl">Abyssinian owl</a> (<i>Asio abyssinicus</i>), native to <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a> especially in the northern area such as <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ioc_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayr_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous2_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Snow_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Snow-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Marsh_owl" title="Marsh owl">marsh owl</a> of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> is outwardly very similar to and likely closely related to the short-eared owl, the <a href="/wiki/Striped_owl" title="Striped owl">striped owl</a> (<i>Asio clamator</i>) is somewhat of an outlier among living <i>Asio</i> species and of mysterious origin, despite being genetically related to the other <i>Asio</i>, does not appear to be a close cousin of other living species.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wink_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wink-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randi_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randi-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Studies of the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial genome</a> found that the <i>Asio</i> genus, and consequently the long-eared owl, diverged most recently among living owl groups from the <a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl"><i>Otus</i> or scops owls</a> genus, with a more distant branch division from the <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> genus.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> A study of the genetic homogeny of long-eared owls in a single roost site was shown to be slightly higher than between different roosts. However, this homogeny is relatively low for a communal roosting bird in general.<sup id="cite_ref-Galeotti2_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galeotti2-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg/220px-Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg/330px-Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg/440px-Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waldohreule_in_freier_Wildbahn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-eared owls from the nominate race in Europe are often quite ochre in hue.</div></div></div>
<p>Currently, the long-eared owl is considered to include three to four subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-owlpages_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-arkive_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arkive-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>A. o. otus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">1758</a>)- This is the nominate subspecies and is distributed throughout the species’ range in the <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>. They may be found as far west as the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Africa">northwestern Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> through as far east as <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Some populations of this race may winter as far south as in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> of the nominate subspecies may range between 263 and 313&#160;mm (10.4 and 12.3&#160;in) and <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> length may be between 132 and 165&#160;mm (5.2 and 6.5&#160;in). Size appears to increase slightly from west to east, with owls in China being about 4% larger winged than those from Europe. Despite there being no known published weights for eastern/Chinese long-eared owls, they appear to obtain the largest sizes within the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In this subspecies, the facial disc tends to be pale ochraceous tawny, rimmed black with relatively short eyebrows that are marked whitish or absent entirely of markings. The erectile ear-tufts are prominent, being colored mainly blackish-brown with tawny edges. The upperparts are ochraceous-tawny, finely peppered with dusky spots and blackish streaks on a grey "veil", while the crown is finely <a href="/wiki/Mottle" title="Mottle">mottled</a> to dusky. The nape and hindneck bear dusky shaft-streaks with the feather outer webs of the scapulars being whitish, forming a row across the shoulder. <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">Primaries</a> basally are uniformly ochraceous-tawny, distally barred light and dark, while the <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">secondaries</a> are barred ochraeous and dusky. The tail is typically tawny with a greyish wash, overlaid with 6-8 very narrow dark brown bars. The underparts have a base colour of ochre, with the foreneck and upper breast marked with blackish-brown streaks; these becomes paler below and marked with dusky shaft-streaks and narrow cross bars. The underwing has distinctive barring and dark comma-like markings at the wrist (conspicuously lacking on overlapping Eurasian <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a>). The eyes tend to be yellowish-<a href="/wiki/Orange_(colour)" title="Orange (colour)">orange</a> to orange, but occasionally may be <a href="/wiki/Chrome_yellow" title="Chrome yellow">chrome yellow</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cere" class="mw-redirect" title="Cere">cere</a> is brownish-<a href="/wiki/Flesh" title="Flesh">flesh</a>, the bill is grey and the talons are blackish-grey. In this subspecies, the downy chick is whitish with pink skin, while the <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_bird_terms" title="Glossary of bird terms">mesoptile plumage</a> is fluffy greyish to brownish-white with diffusely barred dusky flight and tail feathers, being similar to adults but with the ear tufts not yet developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. o. canariensis</i> (<a href="/wiki/Gyula_Madar%C3%A1sz" title="Gyula Madarász">Madarász</a>, 1901)- This subspecies is <a href="/wiki/Endemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endemic">endemic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>. With a <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> measurement of 257 to 284&#160;mm (10.1 to 11.2&#160;in), this is seemingly the smallest subspecies of the long-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This race averages darker than most long-eared owls of the nominate subspecies, bearing heavier and sharper dark markings overall. Furthermore, the Canary Island long-eared owls tend to have brighter reddish-orange eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baez_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baez-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. o. wilsonianus</i> (Lesson, 1830)- This subspecies is opined<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> to include the long-eared owl's entire range in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, distributed from <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a> south to <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, and in the east from <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a> to <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, winters partially down as far south in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> (and seldom in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> typically measures 284 to 305&#160;mm (11.2 to 12.0&#160;in). In general, American long-eared owls are more vividly marked than many Eurasian populations. The facial disc is bright rufous, with a strong blackish rim and extensive white about the disc. The eyes are typically a deep yellow. Meanwhile, the markings on the underside usually are quite blackish and prominent with distinct cross bars. At times, a second subspecies (<i>A. o. tuftsi</i>) is considered in <a href="/wiki/Western_North_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Western North America">western North America</a>, but current data suggests it is weakly differentiated and may compromise <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal variations</a> due to region and habitat, rather than subspecific differences.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayr_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_-California,_USA-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg/220px-Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg/330px-Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg/440px-Asio_otus_-California%2C_USA-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2916" data-file-height="2109" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_-California,_USA-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In most parts of the range, such as here in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, long-eared owls favor stands of conifers adjacent to openings.</div></div></div>
<p>The long-eared owl has an extremely large distributional range. In <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, they are distributed from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> (including almost the entirety of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>), in both of which they are found somewhat spottily but quite broadly, especially for an owl. From western <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> east through the remainder of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> they are found nearly everywhere, though still usually quite common in these areas, there are small spots where they do not usually occur in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">Southeastern Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> they are found only as a breeding species in about the southern two-thirds of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> while they usual persist year around in haunts in the southern tips of Norway and Sweden, respectively, as well as in all of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. In coastal Norway they are found at their worldwide northern limit as breeding bird, with long-eared owls nesting as far north as in the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">Subarctic</a> zone of <a href="/wiki/Troms" title="Troms">Troms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In latitudinal range, they are found as far south the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> while their limited breeding range in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">north Africa</a> is from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> to <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, as well as seemingly in northernmost <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jiguet_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jiguet-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Keith_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Out of Europe, they are found very spottily as breeders in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, northernmost <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jiguet_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jiguet-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Keith_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> They are quite broadly distributed within <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, breeding in about the southern two-thirds of the country (north as far as roughly <a href="/wiki/Chernyshevsky,_Sakha_Republic" title="Chernyshevsky, Sakha Republic">Chernyshevsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yakutsk" title="Yakutsk">Yakutsk</a>) and often occurring year around in about the southern third of it (north to about the cities of <a href="/wiki/Perm" title="Perm">Perm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyumen" title="Tyumen">Tyumen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tomsk" title="Tomsk">Tomsk</a>) and east to <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, ranging far as <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>. Their range is continuous from Russia into most of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, about half of <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and infrequently into northern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In the east, they range through most of <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> (absent from the southwest) and the western and eastern parts of northern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, with seasonally uncertain status in the <a href="/wiki/Koreas" class="mw-redirect" title="Koreas">Koreas</a>. Long-eared owls are found throughout the islands of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> but mainly winter only in points south of <a href="/wiki/Osaka" title="Osaka">Osaka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brazil_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brazil-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The long-eared owl occurs apparently only in winter in small spots of <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, northwestern <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, broadly in much of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (such as the <a href="/wiki/Kutch" class="mw-redirect" title="Kutch">Kutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>), as well as to the east in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and most of <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Keith_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keith-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brazil_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brazil-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Irruptive wanderings have resulted in vagrant long-eared owls in various places such as the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Faroe islands">Faroe islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This species is found widely distributed in North America as well. Their northern limits are reached in much of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, though they are mainly absent from the western and coastal part, with the breeding range scarcely spilling over into the southern part of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_territories" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwest territories">Northwest territories</a>. As is the case in most of Eurasia, they are usually found at up to <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">50 degrees north</a>. Long-eared owls are also found breeding in most of <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, all but northern <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> only to the southernmost part of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Bay" title="Hudson Bay">Hudson Bay</a>. However, with the exception of inland southern British Columbia, southern Alberta, and the southern parts of Ontario and Quebec, as well as an isolated population in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland">Newfoundland</a>, long-eared owls usually vacate their Canadian range during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The species range as a breeding species is far more extensive in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">west</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">east</a> within the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> They breed in <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a> mostly continuously to much of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, where habitat is appropriate, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> and western <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> as well as less broadly in <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>. Even though they are absent from the Pacific coasts in Washington and <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, they may be found breeding along the Pacific in <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">southern California</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Baja_California" title="Baja California">Baja California</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The first record of breeding for mainland Mexico was recorded for an incidental observed nest built by owls in <a href="/wiki/Janos_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Janos Biosphere Reserve">Janos Biosphere Reserve</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)" title="Chihuahua (state)">Chihuahua</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruvalcaba-Ortega_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruvalcaba-Ortega-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> They also breed and occur year around in most of <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>. Breeding and/or year around occurrence is very rare in the eastern U.S. with a few records of them nesting in <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sattler_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sattler-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>  The long-eared owl occurs much more broadly in North America during the non-breeding season and may found essentially all over the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> and as far south in Mexico as <a href="/wiki/Colima" title="Colima">Colima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">Veracruz</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">Oaxaca</a>. The species also occurs in the non-breeding season in <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> (but for the southeast) and much of northern <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> north to <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Very rarely, these birds have turned up in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> (in times of exceptional irruption) and, as a vagrant, even the <a href="/wiki/Bermudas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bermudas">Bermudas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> They are found also by winter and in migration in much of the <a href="/wiki/East_coast_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="East coast of the United States">east coast of the United States</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Banks" title="Outer Banks">Outer Banks</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, broadly in eastern Pennsylvania and almost anywhere in <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a> or <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, southeastern <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> (including <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>) and north to much of southern <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> including almost all of <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> as well as southern <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Optimal habitat tends towards access to open spaces with short vegetation and abundant prey and wooded cover for roosting and nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In terms of attitudinal range, this species may live at many elevations with no strong altitudinal preferences noted, although they tend to be absent above the montane <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a>. The species has been recorded exceptionally nesting at 2,700&#160;m (8,900&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Metres_above_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Metres above sea level">above sea level</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shelley_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shelley-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls tend to inhabit usually rather open landscapes with groups of trees, <a href="/wiki/Hedge" title="Hedge">hedges</a> or <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">small woods</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastureland</a> with rows of trees and <a href="/wiki/Bush_(plant)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bush (plant)">bushes</a>, any type of <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>
