with <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(geography)" title="Clearing (geography)">clearings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">forest edges</a>, semi-open <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swampy areas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a>, especially those with <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orchard" title="Orchard">orchards</a> with old <a href="/wiki/Fruit_tree" title="Fruit tree">fruit trees</a>, parks, <a href="/wiki/Cemetery" title="Cemetery">cemeteries</a> with trees and bushes, even <a href="/wiki/Garden" title="Garden">gardens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">timbered areas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">towns</a> or <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In many parts of the world, including <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">American southwest</a>, long-eared owls have shown the ability to adapt to <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>, though more commonly <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a>, and may nest and roost in available <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oases</a> and adapting to hunt prey over the open desert ground, whether it is <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sandy</a> or more <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shao_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shao-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kotler_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kotler-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Leader_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leader-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stophlet_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stophlet-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The preferred habitat in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> was found to consist most regularly (amongst 200 nests) of small tree <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coppicing" title="Coppicing">copses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grove_(nature)" title="Grove (nature)">scattered trees</a> on <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moorlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heath" title="Heath">heath</a> or <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a> (33%), followed by blocks of <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a> (24.5%), smaller plantations, shelterbelts or hedgerows in various <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural areas</a> (24%) and scrub or wooded clumps near the <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> (15%).<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> All nests in Finland in a study were no more than 500&#160;m (1,600&#160;ft) from cultivated land and only occurred on margins of larger woods or forests.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_management_scheme" title="Environmental management scheme">Ecological compensation areas</a> (i.e. habitat for wildlife on privately owned farmland) in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> did provide habitat for long-eared owls but it was found that <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> were more extensively hunted in mowed sections of the lands rather than the more densely vegetated areas where voles were most abundant. This indicates habitat (in particular open ground habitat) is more important to the predators than prey densities, at least locally.<sup id="cite_ref-Aschwanden_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aschwanden-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, when compared to the <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), long-eared owls were more likely to be found in low disturbance areas where forest transitioned into plantations and to areas with a relatively low human presence.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> A preference by long-eared owls for stands of <a href="/wiki/Conifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Conifer">conifers</a> has been noted in many American studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craighead_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craighead-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, the species breeds most often in dense <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifer</a> stands of wood and reforestation groves that are often somewhat wet, thence less often in mixed or deciduous areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Peck_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peck-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A similar association with conifers was observed aseasonally in <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Craighead_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craighead-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, dense thickets of small trees and brushy margins of more extensive forest tracts were the main habitat for these owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Stewart_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In western areas where mixed woodlands may occur, however, deciduous stands may attract wintering owls, providing they have heavy growths of climbing vines.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevadas</a>, long-eared owls are often found in <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian zone</a> of mixed woods around <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinus_ponderosa" title="Pinus ponderosa">ponderosa pines</a> (<i>Pinus ponderosa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Verner_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Verner-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Analysis from <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> has shown that <a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">forest management</a> had no discernable effect on long-eared owls, indicating that they are not truly forest owls but clearances of riparian vegetation, conversion of foraging areas to agricultural fields and reforestation of open habitats did reduce local numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Holt_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holt-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Despite being adaptable to both very cold areas, including the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> and scarcely <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">Subarctic</a>, and quite warm areas, including drier and/or <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> parts of the <a href="/wiki/Subtropic" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropic">subtropics</a>, the long-eared owl is largely confined to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate zones</a> of the north and is less climatically adaptable than the short-eared owl, with the latter species acclimating to nearly all climates and making its homes both in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> whether wet and dry so long as open habitats are available.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nijman_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nijman-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reid_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reid-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG/220px-Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG/330px-Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG/440px-Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1173" data-file-height="1574" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_uszatka1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-eared owls are usually strictly nocturnal birds.</div></div></div>
<p>Long-eared owls are more or less strictly nocturnal in activity. Usually activity for the species commences at dusk.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> After nightfall long-eared owls in <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> were least active from 8-10 pm and from 5-6 am, while the hours around 10-12 pm and 3-5 am were often the peak times of activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Craig_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> When living relatively close to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, long-eared owls may be forced to forage during daylight as no full nightfall may occur during summer.<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong2-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> When flying by day, long-eared owls are often mobbed by diurnal birds such as <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Often long-eared owls will discharge a fairly large amount of pellets and drop them below regular day roosts. Unlike most other owls, the species has no territorial hunting ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, 14 long-eared owls examined using <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">radiotelemetry</a> were found to have an average home range of 980&#160;ha (3.8&#160;sq&#160;mi). In the study, they required fields along borders of woodlands, avoiding completely treeless areas more so than they were prevalent in the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Henrioux_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henrioux-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/%C4%8Cesk%C3%A9_Bud%C4%9Bjovice" title="České Budějovice">České Budějovice</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, 9 radiotagged owls were studied. Nearly equal numbers were found in suburban and urban areas, and urban ones used developed areas for more than 50% of their nocturnal activity while suburban ones used developed areas for less than half of their activities. Similar habitats were favored by both urban and suburban owls but urban owls had to range wider to avoid heavy human activity and access city parks and so had larger average ranges, 446&#160;ha (1.72&#160;sq&#160;mi) vs 56&#160;ha (0.22&#160;sq&#160;mi), while suburban ones had easier access to meadows and stands of woods.<sup id="cite_ref-Lovy_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lovy-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg/220px-Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg/330px-Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg/440px-Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Long-eared_owl_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-eared owls frequently move south in winter but often stay near cold temperate areas that may hold snow so long as prey remains.</div></div></div>
<p>Out of roughly 19 regular species of owl in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and 13 regular species of owl in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the long-eared owl is classed as one of the five in both continents to be truly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory</a>, moving annually in at least some areas and in some numbers from summer to winter grounds and back whether or not it is an <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">irruptive year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Russell_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Northern populations are migratory, showing a strong tendency to wander south in autumn. Some normally young bird from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> migrate southwest at distances of up to more than 2,000&#160;km (1,200&#160;mi). Central European adults are less migratory, at most merely wandering in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls that breed <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> generally migrate to nearly anywhere in Europe, from <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> to <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a>, though some may disperse as far as <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">north Africa</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lundberg_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lundberg-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, males and females seem to differ slightly in migratory behaviour. Long-eared owls wintering in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> were found to be heavily biased towards females, also there is a bias towards females in winter surveys in other areas such as southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>. At 10 wintering sites in Europe, females were 36% more common than males. The hypothesis posited by those that studied the owls in Denmark is that females face a higher rate of predation by larger birds of prey and may distribute away from <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> where densities are high of those predators and to areas that show low densities of these predators. Another, non-exclusive, theory is that they may be avoiding areas with deep snow that may inhibit prey capture.<sup id="cite_ref-Erritzoe_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erritzoe-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Supporting data that male long-eared owls winter further north than females was gathered in southern <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, where the recovery of dead owls (from <a href="/wiki/Car" title="Car">car</a> or <a href="/wiki/Overhead_power_line" title="Overhead power line">powerline</a> collisions) of the species encountered in late fall throughout winter, males were 45% more common than females.<sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> On evidence, many of the females that vacate <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> come to winter in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> (the female migrants here being up to 3.5 times more common during winter than male migrants in one study).<sup id="cite_ref-Harvey_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvey-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Overskaug_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Overskaug-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Two birds in ringing studies that were recorded in late winter in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> were observed to return for the summer to central Russia (near <a href="/wiki/Yaroslavl" title="Yaroslavl">Yaroslavl</a>, 2,050&#160;km (1,270&#160;mi) away) and to eastern Russia (near <a href="/wiki/Kazan" title="Kazan">Kazan</a>, 2,410&#160;km (1,500&#160;mi) away).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Birds that breed in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central Asia</a> have been recorded to winter in a wide variety of locales, including the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Egyptian Nile valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In North America, migrants generally sourced from <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest" title="Upper Midwest">Upper Midwest</a> winter almost anywhere in the remaining <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, however they will irruptively migrate as far as <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> and several areas of <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and seldomly in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Typically the northern limit of the wintering range fall up to the <a href="/wiki/Okanagan" title="Okanagan">Okanagan Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, the southern reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest" title="Upper Midwest">Upper Midwest</a> and central <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weidensaul_66-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Spring migration was tracked along with <a href="/wiki/Northern_saw-whet_owl" title="Northern saw-whet owl">northern saw-whet owls</a> (<i>Aegolius acadius</i>) as they migrated through <a href="/wiki/Oswego_County,_New_York" title="Oswego County, New York">Oswego County, New York</a> via <a href="/wiki/Mist_net" title="Mist net">mist nets</a>, with the saw-whet considerably outnumbering long-eared owls. Here, migration of long-eared owl was between March 21 and April 14 and, unlike the saw-whets, the long-eared owl migration does not appear to be effected as extensively by weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Slack_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls are 19.5% of owls (or 197 total owls) recorded migrating through <a href="/wiki/Cape_May_Point,_New_Jersey" title="Cape May Point, New Jersey">Cape May Point</a> in fall (against a majority, 60.6%, being saw-whet owls), with 26.1% of the specimens from the species caught in the mist nets being adults. More than 90% of long-eared owls migrate between mid-October and late November, with the immatures migrating earlier, 52.1% of juveniles having passed through in October whereas only 9.4% of adults migrated in the month. Cape May studies also indicated that 58.87% of long-eared owls were caught in the dark before dawn rather than other times of night.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Based on evidence from Cape May, migrating long-eared owls tend to fly higher above the ground than do migrating saw-whet and <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> but not as high as the short-eared owl, with the latter owl often able to avoid <a href="/wiki/Mist_net" title="Mist net">mist nets</a> apparently via its flying height while moving through.<sup id="cite_ref-Sutton_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Per a study in Idaho, 7 radio-tagged owls actually migrating both north, 75 to 125&#160;km (47 to 78&#160;mi) north of their respective nesting sites, and to higher elevations after the breeding season (in many cases possibly to exploit displaced rodents in recently <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logged</a> areas).<sup id="cite_ref-Ulmschneider2_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulmschneider2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The long-eared owl has the peculiar ability to increase populations and then disperse in nearly multi-directional movements during good years for prey numbers. Banding records across North America show highly erratic numbers and movements across the continent of North America with unpredictable peak numbers of migrants in completely different years respectively for the states of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, the species is sometimes considered “nomadic” despite many populations of the species being consistent annual migrants. Similar tendency towards so-called “nomadism” is shared by other widespread raptors semi-specialized to hunting <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> over open ground, such as <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harriers</a> (<i>Circus cyaenus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> These erratic movements and peaks and ebbs of northern populations has lent to descriptions of the long-eared owl as “<a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">irruptive</a>”, however it generally moves in quite different ways from owls largely endemic to the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>), which are more traditionally irruptive in that they tend to migrate little or not at all when prey remain ample in their native home range but then move south in mass when prey populations crash. Long-eared owls, unlike these irruptive northern owls, often migrate from the northern areas regardless of conditions. However, like the northern irruptive owls, long-eared owls tend to appear in unprecedented numbers to the south when a peak prey year is followed by a winter during which prey population crashes. In North America, the migratory habits of long-eared owls are strongly mirrored by those of <a href="/wiki/Northern_saw-whet_owl" title="Northern saw-whet owl">northern saw-whet owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheveau_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheveau-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Newton_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Study of banding records in <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a> show that the long-eared owl Canadian populations may be considered more truly irruptive species both as a breeder and migrant in that only appears in numbers during peak vole years, with large numbers only in 4 of 44 banding years. During 7 low years, the long-eared owls of Saskatchewan seemed to disappear altogether from much of the province. Peak years also coincided often with <a href="/wiki/Snowshoe_hare" title="Snowshoe hare">snowshoe hare</a> (<i>Lepus americanus</i>) peaks, possibly due to lessened competition (as the larger owls favored prey is locally the hare) and interspecific predation by <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston2_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston2-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Houston_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The phenomena of movements that seem to be nomadic or purportedly irruptive in nature may occur as well in Europe, though given the species’ denser population in general there in comparison to North America may result in less noticeable wide variations in numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Years with irruptive numbers of long-eared owls have been noted in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> when prey peaks then crashes back in Scandinavia, resulting in much larger numbers of migrant long-eared owls into the islands than normal, as well as concurrently large numbers of short-eared owls and harriers.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davenport_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> In southern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> during a peak prey year followed by a prey crash, a very large number of long-eared owls were detected and were seen to be likely food stressed, as several were foraging actively during daylight despite the extensive nighttime hours during the season.<sup id="cite_ref-Soikkeli_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soikkeli-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_roosting_behavior">Social and roosting behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Social and roosting behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg/220px-2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg/330px-2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg/440px-2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2976" data-file-height="3968" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2011I30_Muzlja_P1301224_bowls.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A communal roost of long-eared owls.</div></div></div>
<p>During daytime, long-eared owls tends to roost in an upright position on a branch, not infrequently close to the trunk, oftentimes within dense foliage. In winter often stays close to the same tree or grove of trees (i.e. in <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">parks</a>, large gardens, or cemeteries). Usual when approached, the owl will freezes with its body stiffly upright, eyes closed to narrow slits and ear tufts erect. This is called the “tall-thin position” and is common to at least a couple dozen species of typical owl. If approached closed, the owls will alternately open and close their eyes (apparently having stirred but trying to fool potential predators into thinking the owl is still at rest), finally lowering ear tufts, fluffing body plumage and flying to another roost.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Unlike most owls, which show a tendency towards territorial behavior on a fixed range year-around when possible, long-eared owls in the non-breeding season are often prone to occur in <a href="/wiki/Aggregation_(ethology)" title="Aggregation (ethology)">aggregations</a> of owls while roosting. Such grouping may not uncommonly include up to 6 to 50 owls at times, with a European record of about 150 owls at a single roost.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Even other migratory owls in the temperate zone do not tolerate each other as closely as do long-eared owls, with short-eared owls seemingly only forming aggregations when food supplies are exceptionally high, while social roosting in long-eared owls seems to occur regardless of local prey numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schneider_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls tend to roost in the depths of the "darkest stands of trees" in order to conceal their presence, though they prefer being close to forested edge to allow access to hunting over more open ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A study in the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Meadowlands" title="New Jersey Meadowlands">New Jersey Meadowlands</a> area  showed that roosting owls had a strong fidelity for certain trees, particularly <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cedrus" title="Cedrus">cedars</a> where the main truck is large obscured from view and a grouping of at least 2-3 closely clumped trees occurs. Roost height in the New Jersey study was at 3 to 15&#160;m (9.8 to 49.2&#160;ft) or occasionally higher. In New Jersey, different roosts were preferred each year and in the local heavily modified environment, the owls become partially habituated to human activities. However, approach at closer than 3 to 4&#160;m (9.8 to 13.1&#160;ft) away usually caused them to flush. Departure of owls for nighttime hunting would generally occur between 40 and 49 minutes after sundown.<sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Studies in the <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> region of wintering roosts were done over 10 years, with 12 communal and 14 solitary roosts found. The communal roosts included up to 16 individuals per winter, with a mean of 9.9. Overall, the average in Moscow per roost site was 2.1 owl(s). <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">Vole</a> numbers the preceding year were likely the causes of numeric variations in years here while wind and snow avoidance were key in characteristics of roost sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Extraordinarily large roosts were the norm in a study in <a href="/wiki/Stavropol" title="Stavropol">Stavropol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, where the general roost area could host from 80 to 150 individuals each winter over 4 years, with 93.7% of the roosts located in coniferous trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Makarova_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makarova-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, from 2 to 76 long-eared owls were observed per urban roost site. Here, nighttime observation showed that owls individually would depart the roost at peak during the darkest part of nighttime. A majority of the Milan roosting owls did not fly toward urban areas instead flying to suburban fields and forest where prey is more easily encountered.<sup id="cite_ref-Pirovano_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pirovano-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The reason for roosting in aggregations seems to be at least in part to mitigate predation risks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_(Strigidae)_(Long-eared_Owl),_Elst_(Gld),_the_Netherlands.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg/220px-Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg/330px-Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg/440px-Asio_otus_%28Strigidae%29_%28Long-eared_Owl%29%2C_Elst_%28Gld%29%2C_the_Netherlands.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_(Strigidae)_(Long-eared_Owl),_Elst_(Gld),_the_Netherlands.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The talons of a long-eared owl.</div></div></div>
<p>Long-eared owls may divided their hunting into phases, the first stopping around midnight, the second beginning some point after midnight and ending an hour before sunrise.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> While hunting, they tend focuses on the edge of woodlands, hedgerows and open spaces with rough grassland, and over young trees and open ground of all kinds. They hunt mainly on the wing, flying low and fairly slowly, often being quite low to ground, i.e. only  around 150&#160;cm (59&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> More often than visually finding their prey, it is thought that long-eared owls most often audibly detect the rustling of their intended food. In lab settings, long-eared owls were considerably more skilled at finding mice by sound in more or less complete darkness than <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barred_owl" title="Barred owl">barred owls</a> (<i>Strix varia</i>), and in some American experiments even outclassed the auditorily-acute <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> in this regard.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dice_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dice-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Once prey is spotted, the long-eared owl's flight suddenly stalls, then they quickly drop with talons spread to pounce on prey that is perceived, or especially, that comes out into the open.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Habitat may be perhaps even more key than prey numbers to the selection of hunting grounds. Often when prey numbers are more ample in <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">timbered areas</a> nearer the roost or rich <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshy</a> areas in the vicinity, the long-eared owls will still often come to more prey-poor areas such as <a href="/wiki/Field_(agriculture)" title="Field (agriculture)">old fields</a> so that they can detect prey on open ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Aschwanden_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aschwanden-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Getz_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Getz-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The footspan of a long-eared owl, including claws, reaches on average 11.3 and 12.5&#160;cm (4.4 and 4.9&#160;in) in males and females, respectively, which would be large for a <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">diurnal bird of prey</a> but is quite small for an owl of its size, given the physiological differences in the way different birds of prey tend to kill their prey. While <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">acciptrid raptors</a> tend to kill by stabbing with their talons through vital organs, owls are more likely to constrict their prey to death, so tend to have proportionately larger, more robust feet.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fowler_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fowler-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ward_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ward-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In other medium-sized owls, the footspan in tawny owls and barn owls respectively averages 13.4 and 13.2&#160;cm (5.3 and 5.2&#160;in) in between the sexes (notably footspan seems to be a fairly reliable predictor of body mass of an owl as well).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Prey capture by long-eared owls was studied and compared to <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> in a European experiment. The two owl species caught mammals and beetles caught in similar ways but the long-eared owls shied away from flying <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">sparrows</a> and would only capture the sparrows when they perched (though the tawny did not avoid flying sparrows, it too would only capture the prey when perched). Long-eared owls either disregarded or killed but did not eat <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, while tawny owls did catch and eat amphibians when they were available. Occasionally the tawny owls would attack fish whilst one long-eared owl caught but did not eat a fish. The European study further revealed that several owls of different species ate bits of plant and did not appear to do so out of food deprivation or hunger.<sup id="cite_ref-Raber_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raber-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shituan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Shituan.jpg/220px-Shituan.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Shituan.jpg/330px-Shituan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Shituan.jpg/440px-Shituan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1764" data-file-height="1281" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shituan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pellets of long-eared owls.</div></div></div>
<p>Taken as whole, the global population of long-eared owls may appear to have a widely varying diet. One study accrued information from 312 studies from around the species’ range. In total 478 prey species were found to be described, of which 180 were <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammal</a> species, 191 were <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">bird</a> species, 83 were assorted <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> species, 15 were <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, 7 were <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, and reportedly just a couple <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> species. This included a total of approximately 813,033 prey items having been reviewed.<sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> However, on closer inspection, the long-eared owl generally appears to be something of a dietary specialist. It usually takes primarily, often nearly entirely, small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, as food in almost every part of its range. Usually a broad picture emerges that between 80 and 99% of the diet consists of mammals, averaging 94% in one estimate for all of Europe. However, in <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">warmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">insular</a> or more <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">urbanized</a> environments, a greater percentage or, rarely, even a majority of the diet can locally be non-mammalian prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Mean prey sizes have been studied extensively and are almost always fall within a very narrow range. In Europe, the mean estimated prey size overall was 32.2&#160;g (1.14&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, in North America, mean prey sizes have varied between 30.7 and 37&#160;g (1.08 and 1.31&#160;oz) in two estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In general, throughout their range, the mean size of prey is generally encapsulated between 20 and 50&#160;g (0.71 and 1.76&#160;oz), usually well under 40&#160;g (1.4&#160;oz), and only in cases where long-eared owls, perhaps through lessened competition, has regular access to prey weighing 60 to 100&#160;g (2.1 to 3.5&#160;oz) or more, may the mean prey size range uncommonly reach 50 to 60&#160;g (1.8 to 2.1&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Cases of exceptionally large prey are mentioned where they occur below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Topillo_comiendo1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Topillo_comiendo1.jpg/220px-Topillo_comiendo1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Topillo_comiendo1.jpg/330px-Topillo_comiendo1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Topillo_comiendo1.jpg/440px-Topillo_comiendo1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Topillo_comiendo1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Small rodents such as <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common voles</a> are the most important food for long-eared owls.</div></div></div>
<p>The long-eared owls derives almost of its food energy from <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>. Among this order, they are usually associated with a single group, the <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>. The life history of the long-eared owl is inexorably linked to voles. Voles, members of the family <a href="/wiki/Cricetidae" title="Cricetidae">Cricetidae</a> and subfamily <a href="/wiki/Arvicolinae" title="Arvicolinae">Arvicolinae</a>, are often numerous, small-to-medium-sized rodents with relatively short tails.<sup id="cite_ref-Sundell_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sundell-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hafner_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hafner-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, especially, the long-eared owl can be considered a specialized vole hunter. Out of 86 prey studies in the continent, in about 69% voles made up more than half of the prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In particular, in the region of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, a little over 82% of 57,500 prey items was made up of voles.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Where a variety of voles are available in Europe, long-eared owls show a preference for the most gregariously inclined <a href="/wiki/Common_vole" title="Common vole">common vole</a> (<i>Microtus arvalis</i>) over the less sociable <a href="/wiki/Field_vole" title="Field vole">field vole</a> (<i>Microtus agrestis</i>). In central Europe, 76% of the diet was compromised by the common vole species alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rozenfeld_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rozenfeld-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> More specifically, in the largest known study from <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, of 45,439 prey items in the regions of <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nordharz" title="Nordharz">Nordharz</a>, common vole accounted for 72%, with the field vole and <a href="/wiki/Tundra_vole" title="Tundra vole">tundra vole</a> (<i>Microtus oeconomus</i>) collectively another 5.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Another large representation of the common vole was in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, where they made up 84.1% of the diet (27,720 out of 32,192 total prey items). In different years in Slovakia, common voles may range from as much as 92.4% to as little as 57.2% depending on vole numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Tulis_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tulis-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Locally, such as in former <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> and in western <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, about 94-95% of the diet may be common voles alone (of 4153 and 5896 prey items, respectively).<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The dietary association with the common vole generally continues to most areas of <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov2_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov2-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov3_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov3-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> At times, such as indicated in <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, long-eared owls are capable of culling as much as 50% of the common vole population and it was opined that the voles would easily become <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pestilent</a> to humans if not naturally controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-Nistreanu_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nistreanu-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Like many voles, common voles are subject to <a href="/wiki/Population_cycle" title="Population cycle">population cycles</a>. In accordance with the cyclic nature of vole populations, the local numbers of long-eared owls can rise and fall sharply. In low vole years, they tend to lay fewer eggs and feed fewer young, and may not attempt to breed at all. In high vole years, they generally lay and hatch more eggs and rear more young. While incubation starts with first egg, only the oldest siblings may be feed in low food years. Generally various species of <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a> are eaten in poor years for common voles but appear to be an inefficient substitute (at least in more northerly climes) based on the owls’ lower breeding rates.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Apparently, common voles at times may collectively shift in temporal activity into more <a href="/wiki/Diurnality" title="Diurnality">diurnal activity</a> in what may be an attempt to mitigate heavy owl predation (especially as basically all European owls tend to hunt them), although several diurnal predators are nearly as specialized predators of them as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacob_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacob-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Evidence has indicated that common voles are altering their life cycles with unknown long-term results due likely to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. It is likely that the long-eared owls of the region are to be effected by this but it is uncertain exactly what the resulting effect will be.<sup id="cite_ref-Grzedzicka_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grzedzicka-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some parts of Europe, common voles are at times not found or are locally infrequent or rare, especially on large islands, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> and some parts of the southern reaches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. Therefore, long-eared owls live mostly on different prey species. Supplemental or, occasionally, primary prey when voles are less common are <a href="/wiki/Muridae" title="Muridae">murid rodents</a>, especially the commoner genera such as <i><a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">Apodemus</a></i> or field mice, <i><a href="/wiki/Mus_(genus)" title="Mus (genus)">Mus</a></i> or house mice and, occasionally, <i><a href="/wiki/Rattus" title="Rattus">Rattus</a></i> or typical rats.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> studies, common voles were still the main prey in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> where distributed (and outnumbered field voles in the owl's diet four-to-one) and partially in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> (where foods were split between the two commoner vole species and field mice) but are not in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, where common voles are not found, field voles were the main food, making up 65.2% of 13,917 prey items, followed by <i><a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">Apodemus</a></i> field mice species, which were a further 25.3%.<sup id="cite_ref-Kallander_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kallander-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In Norway, 3431 prey items were primarily field voles (42.75%), <i>Apodemus</i> species (12.64%),  <a href="/wiki/Tundra_vole" title="Tundra vole">tundra voles</a> (12.35%) and <a href="/wiki/Bank_vole" title="Bank vole">bank voles</a> (<i>Myodes glareolus</i>) (12.06%).<sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, primary prey switched between field voles (46.6% of 1228 prey items in the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a> and 79% in southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>) and <i>Apodemus</i> mice, most especially the <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a> (<i>Apodemus sylvaticus</i>), (75.6% of 1772 prey items pooled from different parts of England and 69.5% of 1373 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, where native voles are entirely absent).<sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Schmidt_116-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fairley_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairley-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In warmer areas, the long-eared owls may vary in diet depending on local prey composition. In <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, voles, including common voles, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_pine_vole" title="Mediterranean pine vole">Mediterranean pine voles</a> (<i>Microtus duodecimcostatus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Lusitanian_pine_vole" title="Lusitanian pine vole">Lusitanian pine vole</a> (<i>Microtus lusitanicus</i>), collectively made up 76.4% of 6945 prey items in the central part of the country while on the <a href="/wiki/Ebro" title="Ebro">Ebro</a> in northern Spain, the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_mouse" title="Algerian mouse">Algerian mouse</a> (<i>Mus spretus</i>) was dominant, at 69.5% of 846 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Araujo_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Araujo-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Escala_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Escala-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Overall, in 7 studies from assorted Spanish locations, wood mice and <i><a href="/wiki/Mus_(genus)" title="Mus (genus)">Mus</a></i> species were the most regular prey (together accounting for just under 60% of the sum total prey items).<sup id="cite_ref-Escala_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Escala-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Many studies in Italy show that the species is not as much of a specialized feeder in the country with considerable regional variation in prey preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-Bertolino_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bertolino-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">Inclement weather</a> in particular, including any kind of <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Beaufort_scale" title="Beaufort scale">high winds</a>, seems to cause Italian long-eared owls to increase the diversity of prey that's routinely caught.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubolini_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubolini-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In some Italian studies, <a href="/wiki/Savi%27s_pine_vole" title="Savi&#39;s pine vole">Savi's pine vole</a> (<i>Microtus savii</i>) were the main food, at <a href="/wiki/Prignano_Cilento" title="Prignano Cilento">Prignano Cilento</a> where they made up 60.4% by number and 61.6% by <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>. In others, the <a href="/wiki/Wood_mouse" title="Wood mouse">wood mouse</a> was the main food, such as <a href="/wiki/Cremona" title="Cremona">Cremona</a>, at 59.1% of 1482 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Cecere_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cecere-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Masutti_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Masutti-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> An unusual close prey association was noted in northern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, where access to <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a> allowed them access to exceptionally large prey, <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">brown rat</a> (<i>Rattus norvegicus</i>), with juvenile rats caught that weighed on average 140&#160;g (4.9&#160;oz) and sometimes weighing up to 243&#160;g (8.6&#160;oz), making up 20.5% by number and 65.1% by biomass, although wood mice were the most numerous found prey in pellets. Because of the access to rats, the mean prey size in the north Italian study was an exceptionally high 58&#160;g (2.0&#160;oz)<sup id="cite_ref-Pirovano2_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pirovano2-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Generally, <i>Mus</i> mice seem to be the main foods for long-eared owls in Greece, especially the <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_mouse" title="Macedonian mouse">Macedonian mouse</a> (<i>Mus macedonicus</i>), but also not infrequently the <a href="/wiki/Southern_vole" title="Southern vole">southern vole</a> (<i>Microtus levis</i>) is important in the diet there as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kafkaletou-Diez_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kafkaletou-Diez-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, the introduced <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">house mouse</a> (<i>Mus musculus</i>) was deemed to primarily support the owls today, consisting of 69.5% of 3628 prey items per the largest known study.<sup id="cite_ref-Trujillo_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of Europe within Eurasia, the feeding association with voles for long-eared owls weakens somewhat but does continue apiece in adjacent <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, particularly southern vole and <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther%27s_vole" title="Günther&#39;s vole">Günther's vole</a> (<i>Microtus guentheri</i>) (the latter, for instance, making up to 78.7% of 5324 prey items in <a href="/wiki/Karap%C4%B1nar" title="Karapınar">Karapınar</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Seckin_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seckin-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selcuk_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selcuk-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hizal_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hizal-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, prey preferences varied based on <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> composition in <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">desert edge areas</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> studies showing primary shifting rapidly from <i><a href="/wiki/Gerbillus" title="Gerbillus">Gerbillus</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Gerbil" title="Gerbil">gerbils</a> species to <i><a href="/wiki/Meriones_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Meriones (genus)">Meriones</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Jird" class="mw-redirect" title="Jird">jirds</a> to Günther's voles, with similar findings in wintering owls in Iran.<sup id="cite_ref-Leader_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leader-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charter_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charter-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khaleghizadeh_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khaleghizadeh-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Relatively large-sized prey, <a href="/wiki/Indian_gerbil" title="Indian gerbil">Indian gerbils</a> (<i>Tatera indica</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Short-tailed_bandicoot_rat" title="Short-tailed bandicoot rat">short-tailed bandicoot rats</a> (<i>Nesokia indica</i>),  was reported for wintering long-eared owls in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> estimated to average 163&#160;g (5.7&#160;oz) and 155&#160;g (5.5&#160;oz), respectively, constituted a good portion of the prey (72.9% of biomass) and taken in almost even numbers with smaller <i><a href="/wiki/Gerbillus" title="Gerbillus">Gerbillus</a></i> species.<sup id="cite_ref-Khaleghizadeh_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khaleghizadeh-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Much further east, in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, long-eared owls often subsist on different varieties rodent, especially small <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a>, which are also cricetid but not arvicoline, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_striped_hamster" title="Chinese striped hamster">Chinese striped</a> (<i>Cricetulus barabensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Roborovski_dwarf_hamster" title="Roborovski dwarf hamster">Roborovski dwarves</a> (<i>Phodopus roborovskii</i>), alternatively with larger jirds, such as <a href="/wiki/Midday_jird" title="Midday jird">midday jirds</a> (<i>Meriones meridianus</i>), as well as <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">house mice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shao_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shao-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Liu_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liu-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stubbe_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stubbe-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In more northerly eastern regions, voles continue to be of import. In western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, tundra voles, <a href="/wiki/Narrow-headed_vole" title="Narrow-headed vole">narrow-headed voles</a> (<i>Microtus gregalis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_harvest_mouse" title="Eurasian harvest mouse">Eurasian harvest mouse</a> (<i>Micromys minutus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Steppe_lemming" title="Steppe lemming">steppe lemming</a> (<i>Lagurus lagurus</i>) were the main rodent prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Dupal_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dupal-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, diet is strongly biased to rodents such as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_grass_vole" title="Japanese grass vole">Japanese grass vole</a> (<i>Microtus montebelli</i>) (84.2% of foods in <a href="/wiki/Niigata_(city)" title="Niigata (city)">Niigata</a> on <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>), <a href="/wiki/Grey_red-backed_vole" title="Grey red-backed vole">grey red-backed vole</a> (<i>Myodes rufocanus</i>) (87.2% on <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>) or <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">house mouse</a> (77.7% in <a href="/wiki/Ehime_Prefecture" title="Ehime Prefecture">Ehime Prefecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Chiba_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiba-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Matsuoka_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matsuoka-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kawaguchi_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kawaguchi-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, long-eared owls also primarily rely on small rodents in their diet, but their diet is somewhat more diverse by rodent family and less completely reliant on voles than their Eurasian counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In general, the further north they are found in North America, the more restricted and vole based the diet is for this species. While diets are poorly researched in detail in Canada,<sup id="cite_ref-Munro_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munro-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> in the northern tier states of <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benton_County,_Oregon" title="Benton County, Oregon">northern</a> <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> voles were easily the main prey for long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Getz_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Getz-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holt2_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holt2-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spiker_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spiker-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Christenson_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christenson-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randle_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randle-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morgan_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Meadow_vole" title="Meadow vole">meadow vole</a> (<i>Microtus pennsylvanicus</i>) tends to be a dietary staple, such as in the two of the larger American studies, in Michigan where they constituted 70.6% of 3269 prey items and in Wisconsin where they constituted 83.4% of 3273 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Errington_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Errington-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Another regularly featured vole in the diet in America is the <a href="/wiki/Prairie_vole" title="Prairie vole">prairie vole</a> (<i>Microtus ochrogaster</i>), but few other voles seem to be taken other than opportunistically excluding Oregon where the <a href="/wiki/Gray-tailed_vole" title="Gray-tailed vole">gray-tailed vole</a> (<i>Microtus canicaudus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Townsend%27s_vole" title="Townsend&#39;s vole">Townsend's vole</a> (<i>Microtus townsendii</i>) locally led the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Randle_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randle-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rainey_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rainey-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In North America, non-<a href="/wiki/Arvicolinae" title="Arvicolinae">arvicoline</a> <a href="/wiki/Cricetidae" title="Cricetidae">cricetid rodents</a> such as the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Peromyscus" title="Peromyscus">Peromyscus</a></i>, or deermice, and the smaller <i><a href="/wiki/Reithrodontomys" title="Reithrodontomys">Reithrodontomys</a></i>, or harvest mice, fill the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">niche</a> of small wild mice and may be irresistible to hunting long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <i>Peromyscus</i> mice were the main foods for this species in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> and most studies from <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a> (composing up to 59.1% of the local diet).<sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cahn_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cahn-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Morrissey_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morrissey-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voight_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voight-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In many areas, particular arid vicinities, the <a href="/wiki/Superfamily_(taxonomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Superfamily (taxonomy)">superfamily</a> <a href="/wiki/Geomyoidea" title="Geomyoidea">Geomyoidea</a> supplants cricetid rodents as the primary foods, namely <a href="/wiki/Perognathinae" title="Perognathinae">pocket mice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo_rat" title="Kangaroo rat">kangaroo rats</a> and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Pocket_gopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Pocket gopher">pocket gophers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zapodinae" title="Zapodinae">jumping mice</a>. Particularly this appears to be the case in the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">American southwest</a> where in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Perognathus" title="Perognathus">Perognathus</a></i> pocket mice composed 61.3% of the diet, in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, where species pairs of pocket mice and kangaroo rats composed 51.8% and 20.5% of the foods, respectively, and in <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">southern California</a>, where <i>Perognathus</i> were 51% of foods and <i><a href="/wiki/Dipodomys" class="mw-redirect" title="Dipodomys">Dipodomys</a></i> kangaroo rats were a further 37.8% of the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Stophlet_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stophlet-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrows_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrows-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti3_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti3-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Sonoran_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sonoran desert">Sonoran desert</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, nearly all known prey were geomyoids, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Merriam%27s_kangaroo_rat" title="Merriam&#39;s kangaroo rat">Merriam's kangaroo rat</a> (<i>Dipodomys merriami</i>), which alone made up 74.7% of the foods.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In a well-studied population, <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Plain_(ecoregion)" title="Snake River Plain (ecoregion)">Snake River region</a> of southeastern <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Owyhee_County,_Idaho" title="Owyhee County, Idaho">Owyhee county</a> in the southwest part of the state, geomyoid rodents are usually the most prominent prey, especially the <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin_pocket_mouse" title="Great Basin pocket mouse">Great Basin pocket mouse</a> (<i>Perognathus parvus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ord%27s_kangaroo_rat" title="Ord&#39;s kangaroo rat">Ord's kangaroo rat</a> (<i>Dipodomys ordii</i>) (often supplemented heavily with <i>Peromyscus</i> mice). Due to the relatively large size of kangaroo rats, in Idaho, mean prey size may range up to at least 41&#160;g (1.4&#160;oz)<sup id="cite_ref-Marks2_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks2-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Powers_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Powers-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks3_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks3-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks4_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks4-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In some parts of North America, richer biomass are likely when larger prey takes the primary position, such as <a href="/wiki/Cotton_rat" title="Cotton rat">cotton rats</a>. Cotton rats were the main prey in <a href="/wiki/Janos_Biosphere_Reserve" title="Janos Biosphere Reserve">Janos Biosphere Reserve</a>, Mexico (43.2% by number, 69.1% by biomass) and in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, in the latter slightly outnumbering (36%) the much smaller harvest mice (23%).<sup id="cite_ref-Gonzalez-Rojas_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez-Rojas-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Noland_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noland-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Since the mean body size of <a href="/wiki/Hispid_cotton_rat" title="Hispid cotton rat">hispid cotton rats</a> (<i>Sigmodon hispidus</i>) caught reportedly is around 100&#160;g (3.5&#160;oz), they probably represent a very productive prey resource for long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Noland_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noland-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klippel_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klippel-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, exceptional large prey was taken northeastern <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, where both juvenile, weighing about 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz) and adult, weighing about 90&#160;g (3.2&#160;oz), <a href="/wiki/Northern_pocket_gopher" title="Northern pocket gopher">northern pocket gophers</a> (<i>Thomomys talpoides</i>) were caught and made up 55.7% by number and 74.4% by biomass of the diets of long-eared owls. Depending on circumstances, the mean size of northern pocket gophers taken in different areas can vary from 41 to 100&#160;g (1.4 to 3.5&#160;oz) or higher, but long-eared owls usually take juveniles outside of the Oregon study (weighing on the lower end of that mass scale).<sup id="cite_ref-Craig_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks3_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks3-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craig2_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig2-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bull_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Non-rodent mammalian prey is seldom of great import to long-eared owls, though they can take some numbers of other kinds of mammals locally. Despite claims that the long-eared owls “avoids” <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a> as prey, it is probably more correct to say that they do not seek them out nearly as often as more socially inclined and/or densely populated rodent prey. Some other owls may be considered regular and common shrew predators, such as often <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>. In Europe, a broad picture of prey selection indicates about 2% of the diet of long-eared owls consists of shrews.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Locally, relatively high numbers of shrews were reported in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, where 10.7% of 3759 prey items were <a href="/wiki/Common_shrew" title="Common shrew">common shrews</a> (<i>Sorex araneus</i>), in southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, where the common shrew made up 17.3% of 514 prey items, in northeast <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Lesser_white-toothed_shrew" title="Lesser white-toothed shrew">lesser white-toothed shrew</a> (<i>Crocidura suaveolens</i>)  were 19.3% of 311 prey items and in western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, where <i><a href="/wiki/Sorex" title="Sorex">Sorex</a></i> species were 17.3% of 335 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korpimaki_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korpimaki-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dupal_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dupal-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in North America, shrews are rarely more than secondary prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-7" class="reference"><a
