href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Exceptionally high numbers of shrews were noted in <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, where the relatively large <a href="/wiki/Northern_short-tailed_shrew" title="Northern short-tailed shrew">northern short-tailed shrew</a> (<i>Blarina brevicauda</i>) and the relatively small <a href="/wiki/North_American_least_shrew" title="North American least shrew">North American least shrew</a> (<i>Cryptotis parva</i>) made up 10.46% and 10.22% of the diet, respectively, and in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, where 12.85% of the diet was least shrew.<sup id="cite_ref-Randle_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randle-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Klippel_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klippel-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The smallest mammal on earth (and thus smallest recorded mammalian or vertebrate prey), the 1.8&#160;g (0.063&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_shrew" title="Etruscan shrew">Etruscan shrew</a> (<i>Suncus etruscus</i>), may be seldom hunted by long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Tome_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">Bats</a> are another supplemental prey type for long-eared owls. One compilation study based on 12 study sites in the Mediterranean area (in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>) found up to 2% of prey remains were bats. The bats taken in the Mediterranean region ranged in size from the <a href="/wiki/Whiskered_bat" title="Whiskered bat">whiskered bat</a> (<i>Myotis mystacinus</i>), estimated at as low as 4&#160;g (0.14&#160;oz), to the <a href="/wiki/European_free-tailed_bat" title="European free-tailed bat">European free-tailed bat</a> (<i>Tadarida teniotis</i>), estimated to weigh up to 54&#160;g (1.9&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez2_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez2-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A exceptionally close predatory relationship was noted between bats, especially <a href="/wiki/Japanese_house_bat" title="Japanese house bat">Japanese house bats</a> (<i>Pipistrellus abramus</i>), and long-eared owls in the <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> area of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, where bats accounted for 28.6% of 3561 prey items overall, and 56.6% of the diet locally in urban, rather than suburban, roosts.<sup id="cite_ref-Tian_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tian-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Other mammals, outside of aforementioned groups (i.e. cricetid, murid and geomyoid) rodents, are known to be hunted but are generally a negligible part of the long-eared owl's diet, including <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mole_(animal)" title="Mole (animal)">moles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a> as well as rarely taken rodents like <a href="/wiki/Dormouse" title="Dormouse">dormice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flying_squirrel" title="Flying squirrel">flying squirrels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squirrel" title="Squirrel">squirrels</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Chipmunk" title="Chipmunk">chipmunks</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tulis_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tulis-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Araujo_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Araujo-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stubbe_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stubbe-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> While <a href="/wiki/Lagomorpha" title="Lagomorpha">lagomorphs</a> such as hares and rabbits are very rare prey for long-eared owls, occasionally they may capture one. Such prey was estimated to weigh 271&#160;g (9.6&#160;oz) (for <a href="/wiki/Cottontail_rabbit" title="Cottontail rabbit">cottontail rabbits</a>) and 471&#160;g (1.038&#160;lb) (for <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">jackrabbits</a>) in Idaho, meaning very small young rabbits and jackrabbits are likely captured.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks2_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks2-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks3_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks3-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks4_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks4-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craig2_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig2-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> However, sometimes long-eared owls can very rarely capture exceptionally large lagomorphs. The record sized mammalian prey to be taken by a long-eared owl, was recorded in the case of predation upon a probable juvenile <a href="/wiki/Black-tailed_jackrabbit" title="Black-tailed jackrabbit">black-tailed jackrabbit</a> (<i>Lepus californicus</i>) which weighed an estimated 800&#160;g (1.8&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Two instances of <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenging</a> on <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> were reported in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, the first known case of this for the species, where long-eared owls consumed parts of an adult <a href="/wiki/Crested_porcupine" title="Crested porcupine">crested porcupine</a> (<i>Hystrix cristata</i>) and an adult <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">European pine marten</a> (<i>Martes martes</i>), both prey the long-eared owls were certainly unlikely to have killed.<sup id="cite_ref-Mori_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mori-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_male_(15).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg/220px-Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg/330px-Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg/440px-Passer_domesticus_male_%2815%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passer_domesticus_male_(15).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birds such as <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a> are often taken by long-eared owls in Europe, especially near cities and city roosts.</div></div></div>
<p>Long-eared owls are generally infrequent predators of birds. Food studies from Eurasia place it as an opportunistic and occasional bird predator, while in North America they are do not seem to generally take large numbers of birds in any area. In winter, sometimes these owls can come to live largely off of small birds gathered in communal sleeping places, often near villages or towns. Particularly <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow cover</a> during winter is likely to influence local long-eared owls to switch from mammalian to avian prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Canova_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canova-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Like <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>, long-eared owls have been known to hover around roosts in bushes in attempts to disturb the sleeping birds, which may provoke the prey to fly out of their shelter, only to be caught.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hartley_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hartley-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls, along with migrating <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a>, were observed in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> hunting night-migrating <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerines</a> that were attracted to manmade light sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Canario_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canario-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> When killing birds, long-eared owls are very likely to peck about the rear part of the body and the head and decapitate their victims, resulting in more skeletal damage than is typical in other owls and making prey identification potentially difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-Bochenski_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bochenski-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Particularly often taken by long-eared owls in urban areas and/or the edge of arid habitat are <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a> (<i>Passer domesticus</i>) and occasionally the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> (<i>Passer montanus</i>). A surprisingly high balance of prey for wintering long-eared owls in desert areas was shown to be avian. This was in the case in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, where 37.5% of the diet and 40% of the biomass were avian and the most identified overall prey genus was <i>Passer</i> species, at 20.7% by number and 17% of biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Sekour_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekour-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> For wintering owls in the city of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, 90.7% of the diet (150 prey items) were small birds, led by house sparrow (22%) and the <a href="/wiki/Blackcap" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackcap">blackcap</a> (<i>Sylvia atricapilla</i>) (16.7%).<sup id="cite_ref-Kiat_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiat-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Further study of the long-eared owl's diet in Israel's <a href="/wiki/Negev_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Negev desert">Negev desert</a> showed that 28.3% of 3062 prey items were birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Leader2_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leader2-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, 24.6% of prey items were birds including house sparrow (15.4%) and <a href="/wiki/European_goldfinch" title="European goldfinch">European goldfinch</a> (<i>Carduelis carduelis</i>) (2.4%).<sup id="cite_ref-Handwerk_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Handwerk-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, birds rarely compromise a majority of the foods, but hearty numbers are taken in several areas nonetheless. In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, birds were estimated to contribute an average of 8% of the diet (of 52 species). In peak vole years, birds could be less than 2% of the foods while in vole low years, as much as 33% were made up of by birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in the <a href="/wiki/Business_Park_Sofia" title="Business Park Sofia">Sofia park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, the number of birds, with an average 9.6% by number and 7.9% by biomass,  was variable based on vole numbers but a high number of 44 avian prey species were recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-Milchev_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milchev-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Sonian_Forest" title="Sonian Forest">Sonian Forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, 38.3% of 355 prey items were birds, mostly of various <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> species.<sup id="cite_ref-DeWavrin_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeWavrin-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> A study in <a href="/wiki/Baden-W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Baden-Württemberg">Baden-Württemberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> found birds to compromise 14.75% of 12,890 prey items, a relatively high balance, with the most common identified bird species being the European goldfinch.<sup id="cite_ref-Smettan_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smettan-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Overall British studies found in a sample of 7161 prey items that 1161 were birds (14.95%) and that bird were present in 90% of examined pellets. Of these, 46.9% were house sparrow, 7.5% were <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">common starling</a> (<i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>), 4.65% were <a href="/wiki/Common_blackbird" title="Common blackbird">common blackbird</a> (<i>Turdus merula</i>), 3.35% were <a href="/wiki/European_greenfinch" title="European greenfinch">European greenfinch</a> (<i>Chloris chloris</i>), 2.92% were <a href="/wiki/Song_thrush" title="Song thrush">song thrush</a> (<i>Turdus philomelos</i>), 2.49% were <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_skylark" title="Eurasian skylark">Eurasian skylark</a> (<i>Alauda arvensis</i>) and 2.23% were <a href="/wiki/Common_linnet" title="Common linnet">common linnet</a> (<i>Linaria cannabina</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Glue2_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue2-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> More locally in the <a href="/wiki/Peak_District" title="Peak District">Peak District</a> of England, birds were 23% of the prey by number and 31.3% by biomass. Of the birds examined here, a majority of those identified were <a href="/wiki/Meadow_pipit" title="Meadow pipit">meadow pipits</a> (<i>Anthus pratensis</i>) followed by <a href="/wiki/Twite" title="Twite">twite</a> (<i>Linaria flavirostris</i>), and identified birds ranged in size from a probable <a href="/wiki/Wood_warbler" title="Wood warbler">wood warbler</a> (<i>Phylloscopus sibilatrix</i>) (average adult weight 9.2&#160;g (0.32&#160;oz)) to an adult <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">northern lapwing</a> (<i>Vanellus vanellus</i>) (average adult weight 219&#160;g (7.7&#160;oz)). Furthermore, 80% by number and 11 of 25 bird prey species were characteristic of open habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Italian studies reflect relatively high numbers of birds being hunted, mainly in the non-breeding times. One of the smaller large representations was at <a href="/wiki/Prignano_Cilento" title="Prignano Cilento">Prignano Cilento</a> where 13.85% were made up of mostly unidentified birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Cecere_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cecere-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Regional_Park_of_Decima-Malafede" title="Regional Park of Decima-Malafede">Regional Park of Decima-Malafede</a>, birds were 31.1% of the diet, 13.7% of which were unidentified passerines, while European goldfinches and European greenfinch accounted for a further 7.1% and 4.6% of the prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Trotta_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trotta-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> area, of 642 prey items examined and a total prey mass of 15,038&#160;g (33.153&#160;lb), birds made up 38.47% by number and 41% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Bon_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bon-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> In the winter roost of <a href="/wiki/Imperia" title="Imperia">Imperia</a>, 63.43% of 1020 prey items were birds and 36.57% were mammals. The main prey identified was the <a href="/wiki/Blackcap" class="mw-redirect" title="Blackcap">blackcap</a> (51.6%) while the <a href="/wiki/Chaffinch" class="mw-redirect" title="Chaffinch">chaffinch</a> (<i>Fringilla coelebs</i>) was secondary among avian prey (6.73%).<sup id="cite_ref-Galli_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galli-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romanian</a> studies avian prey was relatively important as well.  In <a href="/wiki/Agigea" title="Agigea">Agigea</a> there, 32.71% of the foods were birds, with <i><a href="/wiki/Carduelis" title="Carduelis">Carduelis</a></i> species combined constituting 6.04% and <a href="/wiki/Swallow_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Swallow (bird)">swallows</a> being secondary such as the <a href="/wiki/Common_house_martin" title="Common house martin">common house martin</a> (<i>Delichon urbicum</i>) (2.52%) and the <a href="/wiki/Barn_swallow" title="Barn swallow">barn swallow</a> (<i>Hirundo rustica</i>) (2.44%).<sup id="cite_ref-Petrescu_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrescu-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Birds were the main foods for wintering long-eared owls in Romania's <a href="/wiki/Danube_delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Danube delta">Danube delta</a>, with birds making up 59.5% of total prey by number and 51.6% by biomass of 948 prey items against 40.7% by number and 48.4% by biomass for mammals. Here, numerous passerines were mostly taken with the <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finch</a> family (18.6%), <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">Old World sparrow</a> family (15.7%) and the <a href="/wiki/Tit_(bird)" title="Tit (bird)">tit</a> family (12.7%) being the commonest prey families among the birds. The mean bird prey size was calculated at 22.2&#160;g (0.78&#160;oz) while the mean mammal prey size was 24&#160;g (0.85&#160;oz). <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">House sparrows</a> accounted for 14.3% of biomass and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_blackbird" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian blackbird">Eurasian blackbirds</a> for 12.3% of the biomass on the Danube.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandor_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandor-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> While mammals usually are dominant in the diets of long-eared owls in Spain, in the <a href="/wiki/Albufera" title="Albufera">Albufera</a> reserve of the nation, birds were 53.5% by number and 48.6% of the biomass of 864 prey items. A total of 34 species of birds were noted, led by <a href="/wiki/Common_chiffchaff" title="Common chiffchaff">common chiffchaff</a> (<i>Phylloscopus collybita</i>) (12.5% by number, 4.8% by biomass), house sparrow (8.2% by number, 12.2% by biomass), barn swallow (6.4% by number, 7% by biomass) and <a href="/wiki/Sand_martin" title="Sand martin">sand martin</a> (<i>Riparia riparia</i>) (3.2% by number, 2.7% by biomass).<sup id="cite_ref-Garcia_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> The main recorded individual prey species in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> was the Eurasian tree sparrow, at 38% of the diet, but other avian prey was negligible here.<sup id="cite_ref-Tian_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tian-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While most of the passerines attacked by long-eared owls are well within typical prey sizes for this species, while pursuing bird prey many reports note that unusually large prey may be attacked. Avian prey weighing around 100&#160;g (3.5&#160;oz) or somewhat higher such as the larger common <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jay" title="Jay">jays</a> is not uncommonly taken. However, unusually large bird prey for long-eared owls in Europe has included several species at least twice as massive, including adults of the following species: <a href="/wiki/Northern_lapwing" title="Northern lapwing">northern lapwings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeon</a> (<i>Columbus palumbus</i>), with an average adult weight of 490&#160;g (1.08&#160;lb), <a href="/wiki/Common_moorhen" title="Common moorhen">common moorhens</a> (<i>Gallinula chloropus</i>), with an average adult weight of 343&#160;g (12.1&#160;oz), <a href="/wiki/Red-legged_partridge" title="Red-legged partridge">red-legged partridges</a> (<i>Alectoris rufa</i>), with an average adult weight of 528&#160;g (1.164&#160;lb), <a href="/wiki/Western_jackdaw" title="Western jackdaw">western jackdaws</a> (<i>Corvus monedula</i>), with an average adult weight of 246&#160;g (8.7&#160;oz) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpies</a> (<i>Pica pica</i>), with an average adult weight of 230&#160;g (8.1&#160;oz). Much of the largest identifiable and provable prey for long-eared owls in Europe overall were found to be birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birrer_110-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birrer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue2_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue2-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sandor_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandor-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Veiga_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veiga-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Despite the relatively scarcity of avian prey in the diet in North America, unusually large avian prey has been reported there as well. Such prey have included adults of <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_crow" title="Northwestern crow">Northwestern crow</a> (<i>Corvus caurinus</i>), averaging 392&#160;g (13.8&#160;oz), two large adult <a href="/wiki/Ruffed_grouse" title="Ruffed grouse">ruffed grouse</a> (<i>Bonasa umbellus</i>), estimated to weigh slightly over 600&#160;g (1.3&#160;lb), and even apparently at least once an adult <a href="/wiki/Sharp-tailed_grouse" title="Sharp-tailed grouse">sharp-tailed grouse</a> (<i>Tympanuchus phasianellus</i>), which average a relatively huge 885&#160;g (1.951&#160;lb) or three times heavier than an average long-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Robinette_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robinette-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sutton2_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sutton2-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grouse_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grouse-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_prey">Other prey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other prey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other than mammals, which compromise a great majority of foods, and birds, which compromise a secondary but locally important portion of the foods, other prey varieties are seldom taken by long-eared owls anywhere. Infrequently, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> such as handful of species of <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and even fewer <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toad" title="Toad">toads</a>. Generally these prey turn up more than singly only as far as is known in slightly arid warmer parts of the species range, mostly within the <a href="/wiki/Canary_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Canary islands">Canary islands</a> and occasionally the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">American southwest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti3_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti3-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carrillo_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carrillo-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Yosef_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yosef-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">Fish</a> are almost never recorded in the diet with a total of two prey fish species, both <a href="/wiki/Cyprinidae" title="Cyprinidae">carp</a> and further unidentified carp, recorded in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov4_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov4-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Despite a rather high diversities of <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> (and a low diversity of other <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> like <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">arachnids</a>) collected overall, especially in different parts of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, they are rarely significant contributors to the long-eared owl's diet. Usually in Europe, if any insects are found in pellets, they tend to contribute less than 2% to the prey numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uttendorfer_114-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uttendorfer-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bohac_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohac-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bezzel_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bezzel-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> Similarly present but slight numbers of insects were noted in Israel as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Leader_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leader-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Exceptionally large contributor of insects, most often a variety of species or genera of <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetle</a>, to prey numbers are noted in some studies such as contributing 6.6% of the diet in <a href="/wiki/Regional_Park_of_Decima-Malafede" title="Regional Park of Decima-Malafede">Regional Park of Decima-Malafede</a> in Italy, 17.5% in northeastern Greece and 13.3% in western <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kafkaletou-Diez_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kafkaletou-Diez-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Amat_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amat-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The record contribution for insects surely for Europe was study in central <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> where a single <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cockchafer" title="Cockchafer">common cockchafer</a> (<i>Melolontha melolontha</i>), was found to constitute 25% of the prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Ciach_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ciach-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Based on a couple studies in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, insects are a common supplemental food there, contributing up to about 17.3% of the prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Sekour_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekour-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Souttou_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Souttou-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> On the isle of <a href="/wiki/Tenerife" title="Tenerife">Tenerife</a> in the Canary islands, an exceptional 33% of the food was recorded to be insects, with both <a href="/wiki/Tettigoniidae" title="Tettigoniidae">bush crickets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gryllus" title="Gryllus">field crickets</a> contributing 14.8% each, although in broader Canary island studies the significance of insects is reduced to 10.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-Trujillo_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delgado_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delgado-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The maximum known contribution of insects to the diet in North America was merely 4.3% of the foods for communal roosting long-eared owls in southeast <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Craig_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/220px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/330px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/440px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2818" data-file-height="3600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Larger owls, especially the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a>, and diurnal raptors can be serious predators of long-eared owls as well as competitors for food.</div></div></div>
<p>The long-eared owl occurs in multiple competitive environments of the temperate zone alongside other <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>. A wide variety of owls are especially likely to be encountered both in terms of shared <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnality</a> and a shared preference for rodent prey, which is favored by about 75% of owls found in North America and about 85% of the owls found in Europe and occasionally favored by nearly all owls in both continents.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Much study has gone into contrasting the ecology of long-eared owls against other owls and also occasionally diurnal birds of prey, especially in terms of differences in dietary habits and habitat preferences. In Europe, arguably the most populous owl is likely the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a>, which has therefore warranted a considerable number of comparisons with long-eared owls. While tawny owls overlap in prey preferences with the long-eared owl, it tends to show greater dietary flexibility, with larger contributions of secondary prey such as birds, reptiles and amphibians and insects. For example, in southern Sweden, the <a href="/wiki/Niche_differentiation" title="Niche differentiation">food niche breadth</a> of tawny owls was roughly three times greater than that of long-eared owls. Generally tawny owls prefer more extensively <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">wooded environments</a> than long-eared owls but can adapt as well as the long-eared to <a href="/wiki/Forest_fragmentation" class="mw-redirect" title="Forest fragmentation">forest fragmentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">peri-urbanisation</a>, thus often competing rather directly over foods. While the tawny owl usually selects slightly larger prey on average than long-eared owls, often over 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz) mean prey mass, studied size of common voles hunted in central Lithuania showed that both long-eared and tawny owls selected larger than average voles, with average caught estimated at 21.45&#160;g (0.757&#160;oz) against the average weight surveyed by humans at 16.42&#160;g (0.579&#160;oz). Surprisingly, especially in the non-breeding season in this study, long-eared owls in Lithuania tended to select larger voles on average (at 21.56&#160;g (0.761&#160;oz)) than did tawny owls (19.56&#160;g (0.690&#160;oz)).<sup id="cite_ref-Raber_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raber-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskas_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskas-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Balciauskiene_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balciauskiene-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nilsson_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nilsson-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Despite the potential for competition and mortality (for long-eared owls) in the interspecific relationship between the tawny and long-eared owls, the proximity of tawny owls in a study from Switzerland appeared to have no deleterious effect on the breeding on the long-eared species.<sup id="cite_ref-Henrioux2_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henrioux2-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> A few studies have examined the habits of the <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a>, probably the next most common European owl and just ahead of the long-eared owl in third, and the long-eared owl in areas where their somewhat overlapping habitat preferences draw them into similar areas. However, the long-eared owl takes larger prey on average usually than little owls, since it focuses more on rodents rather than invertebrates like <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a> and, especially during winter, varies its prey compositions less so than the smaller species. Also, the long-eared owl requires some timbered spots for roosting purposes while little owls can adapt to both treeless and partially treed areas as well as, in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> at least, more heavily modified areas by humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sekour_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekour-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Romanowski_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Romanowski-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Many diurnal raptors in Europe broadly overlap in dietary habits, largely taking <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> where they are available, including most species of <a href="/wiki/Harrier_(bird)" title="Harrier (bird)">harrier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">buzzards</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrels</a> (<i>Falco tinniculus</i>). Other than occasional predatory interactions, competition is limited with most of these diurnal birds of prey due to the temporal differences of their habits.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roulin_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roulin-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lack_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lack-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, several other owls, from ones <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">much smaller</a> than to a few species much larger than a long-eared owl, prefer <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Lemming" title="Lemming">lemmings</a> as prey but often differ considerably in their habitat preferences, distributions, nesting habits and/or hunting habits so the long-eared owls are largely naturally partitioned from competing directly with them.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On a broader scale, both <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> potentially compete for resources with long-eared owls. Despite considerable overlap in both range and diet, long-eared owls are largely buffered from considerable competition with short-eared owls by habitat preferences, as the short-eared owls always venture to more open habitats, generally roosting and nesting in <a href="/wiki/Grass" class="mw-redirect" title="Grass">tall grasses</a> rather than timber, and seldom, if ever, hunt in the same edge habitats as the long-eared species.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Selcuk_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Selcuk-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In western <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> when compared to short-eared owl, long-eared owl showed less of a tendency to appear in cluster where prey was concentrated, most likely because of the habitat differences. In both western Russia and further east in western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, the short-eared tended to take more <a href="/wiki/Tundra_vole" title="Tundra vole">tundra voles</a> in addition to common voles, while long-eared owls focused more exclusively on common voles.<sup id="cite_ref-Dupal_144-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dupal-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Volkov_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Volkov-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Comparisons between barn and long-eared owls have been made in many areas of the latter species less extensive range. The habitats used by the barn and long-eared owls are not mutually exclusive nor are their prey species. Despite being similar aerial hunters of open areas, barn owls differ in life history in many respects from long-eared owls, in part by being cavity nesters. While their dietary habits can appear similar and show similar dependence on small mammals, barn owls are somewhat more generalized and catholic as feeders, with less of a heavy reliance on voles. Barn owls can live quite well on virtually any small mammal assemblage. In some parts of Europe, the two species’ food niche breadth is comparable or even slightly higher in the long-eared. However, globally barn owls are much more wide-ranging, more tropical in their central range and globally have a much wider prey spectrum than long-eared owls. Barn owls are also more widely adaptive to <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">insular</a> living than long-eared owls, and are capable living proficiently on a diversity of prey classes even in environments completely lacking small mammals.<sup id="cite_ref-Yalden_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yalden-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alivizatos_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alivizatos-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sekour_180-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekour-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Leader2_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leader2-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roulin_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roulin-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Capizzi_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Capizzi-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitowski_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitowski-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Petrovici_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrovici-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_barn_owl" title="American barn owl">American barn owl</a> race is larger than the <a href="/wiki/Western_barn_owl" title="Western barn owl">western barn owl</a> race from Europe and relevant parts of Asia, being more comparable in body mass and foot and talon size to the tawny owl while the western race in Europe is roughly intermediate between the tawny and long-eared owls in body size and foot span. Consequently, in the Americas, barn owls tend to consistently take slightly larger prey than long-eared owls and tend to access a broader overall <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">feeding niche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_104-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Barrows_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrows-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks2_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks2-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Despite the great adaptability and range of the barn owl, it was found in a study from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> that they experience higher mortality due to starvation than long-eared and tawny owls. This was attributed to the barn owl's inferior <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_metabolism" title="Fatty acid metabolism">lipid fat reserves</a> while they attempt to survive the cooler seasons within <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climates</a>, as they are better suited to survival in warmer climates.<sup id="cite_ref-Massemin_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massemin-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>North America has more species of owl than Europe and can be considered a more competitive environment for long-eared owls living there. However, again, in most cases habitat preferences, slight partitioning in dietary preferences (which may be in prey species or body sizes of prey selected) and life histories generally allow most species to persist even when living in proximity to one another.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As throughout their range, long-eared owls tend to differ from most other North American owls by being much more migratory in nature. Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Population_cycle" title="Population cycle">cyclic food variations</a> affect them differently and are less likely to be the direct cause of winter mortality than in more permanently residential owls.<sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cheveau_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cheveau-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krebs_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krebs-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Oddly enough, among American owls, long-eared owls most strongly mirror the much smaller <a href="/wiki/Northern_saw-whet_owl" title="Northern saw-whet owl">northern saw-whet owl</a> in distribution, migratory habits and, to a lesser extent, food habits. The main food of saw-whet owls tends to be <i><a href="/wiki/Peromyscus" title="Peromyscus">Peromyscus</a></i> mice where they are available and, like most owls, their rodent food selection can broadly overlap with that of long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Slack_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slack-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Duffy_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Randle_152-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Randle-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> It has been posited that the less broadly ranging occurrence of long-eared owls in general compared to in their European range is in part due to greater resource competition between them and the two widespread <a href="/wiki/Screech_owl" title="Screech owl">screech owls</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_screech_owl" title="Eastern screech owl">eastern</a> (<i>Megascops asio</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Western_screech_owl" title="Western screech owl">western screech owls</a> (<i>Megascops kennicotti</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, the feeding niche tends to be much wider and more opportunistic in screech owls, which often feed on a diversity of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> but also <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a>) and alternate prey (a higher balance of birds and frogs, for instance) and so are unlikely to competitively limit rodent numbers to the detriment of long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti2_111-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti2-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks4_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks4-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most easily observed and most dramatic portion of interspecies owl and raptor ecology is interspecific predations. In this regard, long-eared owls are much more often the victims rather than the predators. Their generally successful life history on the contrary often makes long-eared owls perhaps more vulnerable than most other medium-sized owls to predations and even perhaps many smaller owl species. These include nesting in relatively open abandoned bird nests rather than hard to access cavities. Also, long-eared owls tendency to occur in quite open areas whilst hunting coupled with the loud vocalizations (including begging calls of their fledglings) and other auditory displays are all likely to court predators.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Earhart_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earhart-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Herrera_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herrera-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> In Europe, their most serious predators tend to be the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>). One account recorded 768 instances of predation by eagle-owls and 317 by goshawks (or 55% of the owl prey for recorded for goshawks in Europe).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owls rank in the five most regular predated bird species by eagle-owls in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bayle_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bayle-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forstel_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forstel-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Some biologists feel that long-eared owls tendency to avoid richer prey concentrations in favor of opener habitats and spatial usage, especially while migrating and wintering, is partial dictated by the detection of eagle-owl (and perhaps goshawk) activity, so therefore the eagle-owl has a serious influence on the long-eared owl's life history.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Erritzoe_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erritzoe-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In general, long-eared owls endure predation by a formidable array of diurnal birds of prey in Europe. It is likely that largely perch hunting raptor such as <i><a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">Buteo</a></i> and most <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a> may either chance upon an owl roost during a hunting foray or encounter a long-eared owl perhaps during overlapping activity around dawn or dusk, while large <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a> are likely to only strike one down when an owl is flushed during daytime. Goshawks and other <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i> hawks are a particularly deadly threat among diurnal raptors due to their intensive searching hunting methods and willingness to dive into wooded thickets holding roosting owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sergio2_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio2-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hakkarainen_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hakkarainen-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Beyond goshawks, diurnal raptors in Europe known to be predators of long-eared owls of potentially any age are known to include <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila fasciata</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>), <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>), <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red_kite" title="Red kite">red kite</a> (<i>Milvus milvus</i>), <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">rough-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo lagopus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>) and even (in two cases) the slightly smaller <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayor_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dombrovski_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dombrovski-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vali_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vali-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ivanovsky_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivanovsky-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko2_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko2-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Other than eagle-owls, <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owls</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>) regularly kill long-eared owls where their ranges meet, though in some cases these may begin as territorial attacks by the <i>Strix</i> owls, the smaller long-eared may be consumed regardless.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, the range of predators of long-eared owls is no less daunting. Undoubtedly the most dangerous American predator is the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a>. Similarly as in its European eagle-owl counterpart, horned owls kill long-eared owls regardless of season and conditions. However, despite many accounts of predation, long-eared owls do not generally seem to be as prominent in the diet of great horned owls, at least as so far documented, as they are in the diet of Eurasian eagle-owls. <a href="/wiki/Barred_owl" title="Barred owl">Barred owls</a> can also be somewhat serious predators of long-eared owls as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Errington_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Errington-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bull_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Holt3_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holt3-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Rarer acts of predation on long-eared owls in North America have reportedly been committed by <a href="/wiki/Spotted_owl" title="Spotted owl">spotted owls</a> (<i>Strix occidentalis</i>) and even their cousins, the <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Diurnal predators of long-eared owls including some species that also hunt them in Europe such as golden eagles, northern goshawks and peregrine falcons, as well as <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucocephalus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Cooper%27s_hawk" title="Cooper&#39;s hawk">Cooper's hawk</a> (<i>Accipiter cooperii</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red-tailed_hawk" title="Red-tailed hawk">red-tailed hawk</a> (<i>Buteo jamaicensis</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Red-shouldered_hawk" title="Red-shouldered hawk">red-shouldered hawk</a> (<i>Buteo lineatus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bull_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sherrod_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherrod-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Olendorff_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olendorff-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Though less well documented throughout the range, long-eared owls are also vulnerable to mammalian predators, mainly near the nest. Suspected or confirmed predators in Europe are often <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">European pine martens</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone martens</a> (<i>Martes foina</i>), which are likely to depredate nestlings but also will consume eggs and adults if they are able to ambush them.<sup id="cite_ref-Henrioux2_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henrioux2-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tome2_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome2-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharikov5_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharikov5-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Martens are also a potential threat in North America, as are the <a href="/wiki/North_American_porcupine" title="North American porcupine">North American porcupine</a> (<i>Erethizon dorsatum</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bullsnake" title="Bullsnake">bullsnake</a> (<i>Pituophis catenifer</i>) and, especially, the <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoon</a> (<i>Procyon lotor</i>) (the latter species may semi-regularly kill and eat brooding adult female long-eared owls).<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bull_171-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hayward_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Amstrup_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amstrup-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">Corvids</a>, many of which build the nests long-eared owls use, such as <a href="/wiki/Magpie" title="Magpie">magpies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a> will also semi-regularly raid the long-eared owl's nests and eat the eggs or nestlings.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tome2_237-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome2-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> On the other side of the equation, long-eared owls themselves may infrequently prey on smaller owls. This species has been known to hunt <a href="/wiki/Eastern_screech_owl" title="Eastern screech owl">eastern screech owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_pygmy_owl" title="Eurasian pygmy owl">Eurasian pygmy owls</a> (<i>Glaucidium passerinum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owls</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>), as well as the young of the <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola3_75-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue2_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue2-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg/220px-Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg/330px-Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg/440px-Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Noor_k%C3%B5rvukr%C3%A4ts_tervenisti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young long-eared owl after it has moved to branches near the nest.</div></div></div>
<p>Long-eared owls tend to be monogamous breeders. Non-migratory populations are usually monogamous throughout the year, the pair bond being renewed annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A study in Idaho determined that long-eared owls were locally extremely monogamous, with no extra-pair fertilizations were detected in 59 nestlings from 12 nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks5_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks5-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> There is a single record of a male breeding with 2 females in Netherlands, a very atypical case.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, evidence of <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">polyandry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alloparenting" title="Alloparenting">alloparenting</a> was found in a nest in western Montana where the four offspring of the female owl had two fathers, one of which was related to the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Serial_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serial-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Males claim their territory with singing and display flights with wing clapping.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Singing normally starts at dusk on calm evenings and may continue through the night, clear windless moonlit nights are preferred. The song is usually delivered from a perch, most often at medium height in trees or from the upper half near the canopy, sometimes in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Males are responsive to playback during the period leading up to the breeding season (especially during courtship), to such a degree 45% territories would've been undetected in Spain if males has not responded to recordings (as opposed to listening for spontaneous callings).<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez2_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez2-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Often between 8 and 50 pairs are recorded in different parts of the range in a typical range of 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). In Scotland, about 17% of the 9-18 pairs per 10&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.9&#160;sq&#160;mi) were non-breeders.<sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Typical territories for pairs are 50 to 100&#160;ha (0.19 to 0.39&#160;sq&#160;mi) in Finland when voles are plentiful. Several pairs may nest rather close together nonetheless. The minimal distance is normally around 50 to 150&#160;m (160 to 490&#160;ft) between active nests, but in Idaho a nearest nest distance of only 16&#160;m (52&#160;ft) was recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> When food is abundant, about 10-12 pairs, infrequently as many as 50, may nest within area of 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi). Pairs may tolerate each other in the same patch of trees while nesting if food supplies are abundant.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A study in <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pisa" title="Province of Pisa">Province of Pisa</a> of Italy of 32 territories found 10-15 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) with a mean nest spacing of 1,727&#160;m (5,666&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> In a south-central Idaho study area, pair occurred on average 0.65&#160;km (0.40&#160;mi) apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Thurow_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thurow-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> The highest densities may come from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>. The average density in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> is typically around 10-12 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> However, in <a href="/wiki/Brandenburg" title="Brandenburg">Brandenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, over a 24-year study, it was found the average density was 72.7 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Block_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Block-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Based on studies from Michigan and Wyoming, anywhere from 10 to 100 pairs per 100&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (39&#160;sq&#160;mi) was estimated, with the average range in Wyoming riparian habitat about 55&#160;ha (0.21&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craighead_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craighead-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Densities appeared lower in the <a href="/wiki/Snake_River_Plain_(ecoregion)" title="Snake River Plain (ecoregion)">Snake River area</a> (0.28-0.42 pairs per square km) than elsewhere in southern Idaho (0.64-1.55 pairs per square km).<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Globally, many pairs of long-eared owl occupy the same territory all year around but most prefer to use a different nest each year even if the same nest as last year is still in good standing. Females typically take on the duty of inspecting potential nesting sites and duets with its mate; perched on a chosen nest, she sings to contact the male, later vocal activity is confined to weak calls only heard at short range.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> During displays, females have also have been recorded doing the wing clap display but much less intensely than males.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Copulation tends to occur close to the nest. In Germany, it was recorded that the male precedes copulation with calls and display flights, followed by strong waving wing signals and tilting their body while perched near female and/or the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Copulation has also been observed on both the ground and the trees, preceded frequently by a duet, a male aerial display which ending in descent to ground and a flight by the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Boundary fights between males are unrecorded in this species.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Breeding occurs later in the year than <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatric</a> species such as <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a> perhaps due to their more strongly migratory ways. Rarely mating may occur early as February in cooler temperate areas, but the pairs copulations usually take place in March or April.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Winter breeding detected as new juveniles of 14–18 days of age were recorded in early February in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>. Other prior reports of winter breeding are known from Italy, in two cases, as well as in the Czech Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-Noga_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Noga-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gustin_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gustin-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_(1922)_(14563406738).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg/220px-The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg/330px-The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg/440px-The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_%281922%29_%2814563406738%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2244" data-file-height="1272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_common_hawks_and_owls_of_California_from_the_standpoint_of_the_rancher_(1922)_(14563406738).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An old photo of a mother long-eared owl sitting on her nest.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_(1919)_(14752611561).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg/220px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="211" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg/330px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg/440px-The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_%281919%29_%2814752611561%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1824" data-file-height="1748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_birds_of_the_British_Isles_and_their_eggs_(1919)_(14752611561).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A picture of a rare ground nest in England.</div></div></div>
<p>Normally nests in stick nest of large birds, i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Corvus" title="Corvus">Corvus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Pica_(genus)" title="Pica (genus)">Pica</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">raptors</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ardea_(genus)" title="Ardea (genus)">Ardea</a></i> herons. Other nest builders in Europe can often include <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In Britain and Finland 84% of 239 and 85 nest were made by <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">carrion</a> (<i>Corvus corone</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Hooded_crow" title="Hooded crow">hooded crows</a> (<i>Corvus cornix</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpies</a>. In England and Finland, 77% and 66% of the nest were in <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifers</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In Brandenburg, Germany, 90% of the nest were built by carrion crows and most were in <i><a href="/wiki/Pinus_sylvestris" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinus sylvestris">Pinus sylvestris</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Block_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Block-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In different studies of <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, nearly all known nests were in old <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvid</a> nests (most being in <a href="/wiki/Juniper" title="Juniper">juniper</a>). In one large study, nest heights, which averaged 3.2&#160;m (10&#160;ft), and diameter of the nest construction, which averaged 22.3&#160;cm (8.8&#160;in), were deemed likely to be the most significant criteria for nest selection.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks2_163-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks2-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thurow_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thurow-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> In Ontario, conifers were usually used, often <i><a href="/wiki/Pinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinus">Pinus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Juniperus" class="mw-redirect" title="Juniperus">Juniperus</a></i>, in corvid nests between 2.5 and 18.5&#160;m (8.2 and 60.7&#160;ft) high, but mostly between 5.5 and 9&#160;m (18 and 30&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Peck_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peck-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Nest height averaged 6.7&#160;m (22&#160;ft) in Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In Slovenia, long-eared owls preferred coniferous trees for breeding (in almost exclusively corvid nests), those that nested in <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a> trees had higher nest mortality especially earlier in the season due to higher predation rates.<sup id="cite_ref-Tome2_237-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome2-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, nest site selection was largely correlated to predation risk in Spain, with ivy and tree canopy coverage less important than high scrub cover from below, in part because terrestrial predators were more common in the particular study area than aerial ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Rodriguez_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rodriguez-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> In northeast Switzerland, nesting location are selected for anti-predator features, among 38 nesting sites, the ones seemingly preferred bore denser forest edges, greater canopy cover and were within conifers much more so than were prevalent in the overall environment, while the vicinities of <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">buildings</a> were avoided in the study area.<sup id="cite_ref-Henrioux2_209-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henrioux2-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> An unusual case of urban breeding long-eared owls was observed in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, Russia, where a loose <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colony</a> was observed to form. The Moscow nesting was thought to be the result of <a href="/wiki/Synurbization" title="Synurbization">synanthropization</a> by the owls, in part due to lower predation risk in the urban areas versus rural vicinities (where predation was 6.6% higher). The mean distance of nesting owls in Moscow was 603&#160;m (1,978&#160;ft) whereas it was more than twice the distance in nearby rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Basova_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basova-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Tree nests are usually under 30&#160;m (98&#160;ft) above the ground, and can sometimes be so small that the wings and tail of brooding female may be visible from below.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> More infrequently than avian tree nests, leaf nests of <a href="/wiki/Tree_squirrel" title="Tree squirrel">tree squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawk</a> nests in <a href="/wiki/Saguaro_cactus" class="mw-redirect" title="Saguaro cactus">catci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliff</a> nests of various birds may be used.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Millsap_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millsap-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> While they usually take up already abandoned nest sites, sometimes long-eared owls are capable of chasing off prior occupant of nest even including other raptors (extending to fierce <i>Accipiters</i> such as sparrowhawks, sharp-shinned hawks and even larger Cooper's hawks) indicative of their potential for fierceness and tenacity.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Other than other bird's nests, alternate nesting sites have been used but appear to be usually rare or uncommon.
