Among these, are shallow depressions on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BNA_28-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BNA-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Some ground nest locations recorded (in Europe) have included among <a href="/wiki/Ericaceae" title="Ericaceae">heather</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bracken" title="Bracken">bracken</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubus" title="Rubus">bramble</a>, and even on <a href="/wiki/Reed_bed" title="Reed bed">reed beds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren" title="Warren">rabbit warrens</a>. Some nests have been recorded in <a href="/wiki/Wicker" title="Wicker">wicker</a> <a href="/wiki/Basket" title="Basket">baskets</a> placed in trees for <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grunwald_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grunwald-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mingozzi_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mingozzi-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hosking_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hosking-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In North America, pairs of ground nest were found in each west-central Montana and in Okavagan, British Columbia, in all cases between the roots of or the ground immediate adjacent to the base of trees or bushes (with two other historic records of ground nesting in North America).<sup id="cite_ref-Bull_171-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maples_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maples-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> Artificial nesting platforms made of twigs for the owls are also locally accepted. In <a href="/wiki/Woodwalton_Fen" title="Woodwalton Fen">Woodwalton Fen</a> reserve of eastern England, 71 nests were built in wicker baskets set out for owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Garner_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garner-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Also in <a href="/wiki/Yizre%27el" title="Yizre&#39;el">Yizre'el</a> in Israel, 6 of 16 nest baskets hung in <i><a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">Eucalyptus</a></i> were used by long-eared owls, with all occupied by February. In this general area of Israel, at least 72 other nest baskets for long-eared owls have been set out, to encourage the rodent controlling birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Charter2_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charter2-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>  Exceptionally, long-eared owls have nested in shallow cavities, in hollow willows or oaks, tree stumps or holes in cliffsides, however as a rule they tend to not be cavity nesters.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haverschmidt_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haverschmidt-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> 6.5% of 153 nests in Great Britain were on natural surfaces (mostly the ground) rather than animal nests.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Nesting vicinities where previous attempts were successful are more likely to be reused, i.e. in Idaho where 48% of prior nesting areas were reused after successful attempts.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Egg-laying is between normally between late March and early May in most of the range. Egg laying dates in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">northeastern United States</a>, in a sample of 42 eggs, were between March 14 and May 30. Additional records in southern Canada show laying as occurring at as late as June 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> 43 eggs in Ontario were laid between March 19 to May 24, more than half between April 15 and May 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Peck_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peck-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In northern Italy, the mean egg laying date was March 27.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_229-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Exceptional eggs were recorded as early as December 31 to January 3 in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Corral_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corral-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Re-nesting can occur within about 20 days after a clutch is lost.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In Europe up to two broods have been successfully been raised at times of food abundance.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Generally the female lays 3-5 eggs (sometimes more if food is unusually abundant).  Larger clutches are typical further north in the range.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Record sized clutches have been recorded for Europe of up to 8 eggs in <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> in years of a “vole plague” while record sized clutches worldwide were recorded of 10–11 in the <a href="/wiki/Kazan" title="Kazan">Kazan</a> area of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in similar conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dementiev_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dementiev-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, the average clutch size was recorded as 3.9 while in Germany it was 5.5 and, similarly, in Slovenia it was 5.6.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tome3_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome3-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In a study from Montana, the mean clutch size was 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> The pure white eggs are 40.2&#160;mm ×&#160;32.5&#160;mm (1.58&#160;in ×&#160;1.28&#160;in) on average in both North America and central Europe and weight about 23&#160;g (0.81&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Makatsch_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Makatsch-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> At 1-5 day intervals (on average 2), the eggs are laid on the bottom of nesting area. A clutch of 7 eggs takes 10 to 11 days to lay.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Incubation begins with the first egg and continues for about 27–28 days (in extreme cases from about 21 to 30 days).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craig2_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig2-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The female alone incubates while the male provides food, which is brought direct to nest. She may leave the nest early on to feed but does so much less later into incubation.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> A study in Montana found that <a href="/wiki/Corticosterone" title="Corticosterone">corticosterone</a> levels were considerably higher in adults of both sexes during the breeding season than during the non-breeding season, suggesting that the breeding season is more stressful on the owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Romero_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Romero-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The young hatch at 2 day intervals at any point between very late April and June.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Craig2_170-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig2-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The average weight of 52 hatchlings in Montana was 18.4&#160;g (0.65&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Their eyes open at 5–7 days (averaging 6.4 days in Montana) and they are brooded by the mother for about 2 weeks, often while the male perches nearby and watches over. The female alone feeds the chicks. In Idaho, the male was recorded to 2.5 times more prey deliveries during nesting than did the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ulmschneider2_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulmschneider2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Injury feigning displays have been recorded at the nestling stage as an anti-predation measure.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong_48-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Usually long-eared owls are less bold in nest defense than some other owls, <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> owls for example, but they are capable of fierce protective attack nonetheless. Nesting defense by parents increased in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> further into breeding season, with older nestlings being defended more vigorously. Females do a majority of nest defenses. Owl pairs incurring higher levels of regular disturbance are more likely to have a milder nest defense.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ulmschneider2_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulmschneider2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> During a defensive display, the parent ruffles up their plumage and partially spreads the wings to half open, trampling from one foot to the other, hissing and bill snapping and can look surprisingly large in this posture. If a perceived threat continues towards the owls, they may leap up and try to rake and grab at the threat with their talons. Even animals as large as humans may find themselves the victim of long-eared owl defensive attacks if they approach or, especially, if they climb up to a nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The young leave the nest at 20–27 days of age (average about 22 days) but are initially flightless, often climbing about surrounding branches. At this stage they may be referred to as "branchers".<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tome3_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome3-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Quite often the young fall to the ground, but they are usually able to climb back up using their claws and bill with heavy wing flapping. At dusk the begging branchers call to their parents with high pitched notes to indicate their location. At about 35–37 days, they are fully fledged and can fly well, but they often follow their parents and are fed by them for up to about 2 months, continuing to make high pitched calls.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> In Slovenia, independence was gained about 50–80 days after hatching, while in Idaho it was about 45 to 56 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Ulmschneider2_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ulmschneider2-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tome4_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome4-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Asio_otus_01.JPG/220px-Asio_otus_01.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Asio_otus_01.JPG/330px-Asio_otus_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Asio_otus_01.JPG/440px-Asio_otus_01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young long-eared owl engaging in a threat display.</div></div></div>
<p>Normally in North  America the species produces one clutch per year, but 2 clutches in a year have been recorded in high vole years.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> An exceptional double brood was recorded in Idaho due to high food availability, allowing the pair to successfully raise all 11 nestlings to fledgling, while in same season 3 other females in same grove were able to produce an average of 5.3 fledglings in their single broods.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks6_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks6-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> Like other species using open nests, rather than enclosed cavities, the species has relatively short fledging period and quickly moves away from the dangerous situation of the nest site.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Nesting success averaged 46% between two study years in a study of 112 nests in Idaho with raccoon predation considered the most serious cause of nesting failure.<sup id="cite_ref-Marks_12-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marks-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> A different Idaho study of 24 nests showed that the owls fledged an average of 3.7 young per nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Thurow_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thurow-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> In Montana, the mean number hatched per nest was 3.8 and mean number to have fledged per nest was approximately 2.2.<sup id="cite_ref-Seidensticker_261-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seidensticker-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> 59% of 78 nesting attempts failed in Britain, with an average clutch size per successful pair of 3.91 to 4.53. 41 of 78 continuously monitored in this British study nest successfully produced 1 or more fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>  In another British study, of 58 monitored pairs over 4 years, 83% laid eggs, 63% hatched one or more young, and 57% fledged young, with an average of 3.2 young fledged per successful nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In yet another English study, this time exclusively of owls using wicker baskets, 50.7% of 71 attempts managed to fledge young. Among the wicker basket users, earlier nesters (i.e. March-early April) raised larger broods and had more fledglings than later (late April-early May), in part due to rising water tables making prey capture less ideal later in nesting season.<sup id="cite_ref-Garner_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Garner-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> For 6 pairs of long-eared owls using wicker baskets in Israel, the mean brood size was 3.6 and mean number of young owls to leave the nest was 3.<sup id="cite_ref-Charter2_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charter2-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> At all stages and in all regions, reproduction tends to be more successful when prey populations are higher.<sup id="cite_ref-Village_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Village-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In continental Europe, conditions are better than in England or Finland, perhaps due to the large population of common voles there that are absent in the more northerly countries, and nesting success averages higher.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnsgard_8-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnsgard-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_20-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> However, breeding success still is reliant on prey populations. In an 15&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (5.8&#160;sq&#160;mi) area of southern Germany, one cold spring with few voles no breeding pairs were found. A year later, with a warm spring and many voles, 19 breeding pairs took up residence in the study area.<sup id="cite_ref-Rockenbauch_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rockenbauch-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> First year mortality of long-eared owls has been calculated in Germany as 52% and 31%.<sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In Brandenburg, Germany, of 867 breeding attempts, 36.6% (335) pairs were successful in raising 1468 young, which equates to 1.57 fledgling per all pairs that attempted, 4.31 per successful pair. The Brandenburg data shows that after 1990, when conservation minded farming initiative began, numbers increased significantly. Also in Brandenburg, in one case, 2 successive females to the same male were killed, the male mate was able to pair with a third female and resulting in a late successful fledging (August 24).<sup id="cite_ref-Block_245-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Block-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In Slovenia, as studied between 1984 and 1993, of 79 nests, 32 (40%) produced young, 37 (47%) failed completely, and 10 (12%) failed after hatching. The Slovenian average number of owls hatched was 2.4 per nest (5.3 per successful nest) and the average number of owls fledged was 1.6 per nest (3.9 per successful nest).<sup id="cite_ref-Tome3_260-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome3-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pisa" title="Province of Pisa">Pisa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, long-eared owl pairs produced a mean of 0.95 per territorial pair and 2.13 per successful pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Sergio_229-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sergio-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> In central Slovenia, 57 nests produced an average of 5.7 eggs per clutch. Of the 51 fledged owls, 31 died in the study, 22 of which were due to mammal and bird predation, 6 due to starvation, 2 due to road collisions and 1 drowned in ditch.<sup id="cite_ref-Tome3_260-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tome3-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> In Britain, the most common diagnosed cause of nest failure was egg theft by humans (accounting for 28.2% of 46 failed nests).<sup id="cite_ref-Glue_13-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glue-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Banding studies show that the long-eared owl typically have a short lifespan, with more than 91% of 105 owls recovered in North America of determined age being 4 years or younger. The oldest recorded in these efforts was banded in New York and recovered in Ontario at the age of 11 years and 4 months. Another one may have been over 15 years old, however.<sup id="cite_ref-Houston_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houston-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> One exceptional isolated record is known of a European long-eared owl of just under 28 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Annual survivorship in Germany and Switzerland for adults is 69%.<sup id="cite_ref-Glutz_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glutz-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
 <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG/220px-Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG/330px-Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG/440px-Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3892" data-file-height="2840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_MWNH_2203.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eggs, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The long-eared owl is rather common and widespread in many regions. With a range of 80 million square kilometers, it is easily one of the most widely found owls. The <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> estimates the total population between 2 million and 5.5 million, placing it as one of the most numerous owls beyond the wider ranging <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> and less wide-ranging <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> (both likely between 5 and 10 million total birds) and roughly equaling the total population of great horned owls.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sauer_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sauer-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> The long-eared owl is more numerous than its more scarce but more wide-ranging cousin, the short-eared owl, with the peak number possible of short-ears roughly equivalent to the minimum number of long-ears.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Their range of limit for breeding is typically in Mediterranean type arid, hot climates, where terrestrial insects and reptiles may prevail over small, sociable rodents, the long-eared owl is cut-off, whereas these are peak areas for <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owls</a>. In the north, it is scarce to absent in areas of the deep <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">boreal forest</a> and the treeless northern areas such as around large <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a> or lower <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, where the short-eared owl tends to supplant them.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Densities of populations depend on availability of food. Local threats are typically <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a>. Out of myth and ignorance, this species has long been persecuted by humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hume_19-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In many areas, some humans may carelessly shoot at any crow-like nest in order to destroy the contents, out of resentment for the crow's perception as a pest.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In North America, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunters</a> historically often shot long-eared owls as all owls were badly persecuted. Even though it was perceived early in the 20th century that the species is actually beneficial to human interests, many hunters continued to shoot them, claiming that they represent young individuals of the then nationally despised great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Bent_81-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bent-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Errington_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Errington-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_236-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> Largely in correlation with persecution, long-eared owls were recorded as formerly abundant and then rare in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Wetmore_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wetmore-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Long-eared owl potentially face lethal <a href="/wiki/Contamination" title="Contamination">contamination</a> with heavy metals such as <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biocide" title="Biocide">organic biocides</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rodenticide" title="Rodenticide">rodenticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">PCBs</a> recorded. Pesticides seem to effect the species less than those predators with more varied diets and those that eat carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> High levels of contamination were found in long-eared owl pellets in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, with the concentrations of pollutants in prey that are sourced from industrial and agricultural practices in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Demajo_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demajo-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Like many birds, they may be vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worm" title="Parasitic worm">helminths</a>, which are probably underrated as a potential source of mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_269-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferrer_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrer-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Other parasites and <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> may reduce health of populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Skoracki_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skoracki-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a> has been the recorded source of mortality for some long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Komar_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Komar-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Molina-Lopez_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Molina-Lopez-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anticoagulant" title="Anticoagulant">Anticoagulants</a> may also threaten this species.<sup id="cite_ref-Stone_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stone-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> In the Canary islands, it has become increasingly scarce due to <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">loss of habitat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_3-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Many long-eared owls are also killed by <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">road traffic</a>. High owl mortality were particularly noted along roadways in France, especially due to high common vole numerous in roadside areas, proposed that letting vegetation grow may mitigate some mortalities.<sup id="cite_ref-Baudvin_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baudvin-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> During 1963–1995, 128 dead long-eared owls turned in England, 89 were females and 34 were males. 61% turned had died from collisions (40% from those with vehicles). In this study from England, high levels of <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene" title="Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene">DDE</a> (metabolite of <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Dieldrin" title="Dieldrin">HEOD</a> found in long-eared owls prior to 1977, similar levels to those seen in falcons there, lessened in later samples but 2 birds still had fatal levels of pesticide contamination.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyllie_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyllie-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> In areas such as Switzerland, to offset persecution and increase survival as well as to allow observers to watch them more easily, wintering owls in towns and cities were fed daily white lab mice.<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Breeding can be promoted by artificial platforms of twigs in bushes or trees. Also augmented feeding may ensure survival during low food winters.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_7-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> An attempt to release captive raised long-eared owl in the wild in Italy was largely unsuccessful. Of these, 3 of 8 attempted owls seemed to disperse successfully while the others died or disappeared, while 8 tawny owls released all but 1 seemed to successfully disperse.<sup id="cite_ref-Csermely2_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Csermely2-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> The species has actually increased in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, rising from 25 records over 12 decades to 49 records in two decades (between late 70s and 1997). Now breeding confirmed in up to 12 regions for the Persian breeding population and between 1997-2014 there were 32 non-breeding and 17 breeding records.<sup id="cite_ref-Khaleghizadeh2_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khaleghizadeh2-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">southern California</a> (<a href="/wiki/San_Diego_county" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego county">San Diego county</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">Orange county</a>), long-eared owls are thought to have lost more than 55% of their range due to habitat alterations.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloom_236-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> A decreasing trend has been observed in wintering owls in <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, with 9 of 58 known roosts entirely eliminated due to land development, while the remaining 49 have shown reductions or are no longer used over the course of 30 years. Land use changes and habitat destruction accounts for reduced habitat quality and lower vole populations, with more adaptable raptors such as great horned owls and hawks exploiting much of what remains to the detriment of long-eared owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Bosakowski2_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bosakowski2-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> A similar reduction was noted over 20 years in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Broader studies of banding across Canada, gathered during long term monitoring from consistent annual bird counts from 1966–1992, showed that long-eared owl has declined relatively significantly. A net total 0.98% decline was recorded over the survey time. This was considered the second greatest reduction behind <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burrowing_owl" title="Burrowing owl">burrowing owl</a> (<i>Athene cunicularia</i>), furthermore among the 19 raptors surveyed in Canada, these 3 owls showed the most declining trends. The long-eared owl was apparently the most scarce of the 6 sub-boreal owl species surveyed. A similar trend has been detected throughout North America with a very large net 1.6% reduction overall during <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Bird_Count" title="Christmas Bird Count">Christmas Bird Counts</a> (CBC), again making it the most severe decline for an owl behind only the short-eared owl and the two related owl species seemed to have had the most severe declines of all 28 raptor species mentioned in these CBC surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-Kirk_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirk-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_art">In art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Long-eared_owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: In art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_James_Audubon" title="John James Audubon">John James Audubon</a> illustrates the "Long-eared Owl - <i>Strix otus</i>" as Plate 383 in Birds of America, published London, 1827–38. The print was engraved by Robert Havell in 1837. The original watercolour was purchased from Audubon's destitute widow by The New York History Society where it remained until 9/11, when it was destroyed.
</p>
